(القمر) الاطراد إلخ أي كلما صدق عليه الحد صدق عليه المحدود.(السنبلي) خصوص الجنس إلخ إن كان اللفظ مشتملاً على كثيرين متفاوتين في أحكام الشرع، أو خصوص النوع: إن كان مشتملاً على كثيرين متفقين في الحكم، أو خصوص العين: إن كان له معنى واحد حقيقة. (إفاضة الأنوار)

تقسيم للخاص بعد بيان تعريفه أي الخصوص الذي يفهم في ضمن الخاص إما أن يكون خصوص الجنس بأن يكون جنسه خاصا بحسب المعنى، وإن يكن ما صدق عليه متعددًا، أو خصوص النوع على هذه الوتيرة، أو خصوص العين أي الشخص المعين، وهذا أخص الخاص والجنس عندهم عبارة عن كلي مقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالأغراض دون الحقائق كما ذهب إليه المنطقيون والنوع عندهم كلي مقول على كثيرين متفقين بالأغراض دون الحقائق كما هو رأي المنطقيين، فهم إنما يبحثون عن الأغراض دون الحقائق، فرب نوع عند المنطقيين جنس عند الفقهاء كما يظهر عن الأمثلة التي ذكرها كالإنسان بقوله: كإنسان ورجل وزيد فالإنسان نظير خاص الجنس، فإنه مقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالأغراض، فإن تحته رجلاً وامرأة، والغرض من خلقة الرجل هو كونه نبيًا وإمامًا، وشاهدًا في الحدود والقصاص، ومقيمًا للجمعة والأعياد ونحوه، والغرض من المرأة كونها مستفرشة آتية بالولد مدبرةً لحوائج البيت وغير ذلك. كالإرضاع ككونه ناكحا والرجل نظير خاص النوع، فإنه مقول على كثيرين متفقين بالأغراض، بأن يكون جنسه إلخ الصواب أن يقول: بأن يكون جنسًا خاصا إلخ (القمر) بأن يكون جنسه إلخ: هذا أيضًا دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن كون الجنس خاصا لا يتصور، لأن الجنس ينبئ عن التكثر والخاص على التوحد والانفراد وتقرير الدفع أن التكثر والانفراد إنما يتناقضان إذا كانا بحيثية واحدة وههنا ليس كذلك، فإن التكثر باعتبار المصداقات والمدلولات والوحدة والانفراد باعتبار المعنى والمفهوم فلا تناقض (السنبلي) علي هذه الوتيرة أي يكون نوعا خاصا بحسب المعنى. (القمر) أي الشخص إلخ تفسير للخاص بخصوص العين. (القمر) وهذا أي الخاص بخصوص العين. (القمر) كما ذهب إلخ مرتبط بالمنفي، وقس عليه قوله الآتي كما هو رأي .إلخ (القمر) فهم أي الأصوليون إنما يبحثون عن الأغراض، لأن مقصودهم معرفة الأحكام دون الحقائق. (القمر) هو كونه نبيًا : فيه إيماء إلى أن النبوة تختص بالرجال وما كانت امرأة نبيّة والتفصيل في حاشيتنا على شرح العقائد المسماة " بحل المعاقد ". (القمر) مستفرشة إلخ الاستفراش استمتاع الرجل من المرأة. (السنبلي)
Topic of the Specific. Definition of the Specific and its divisions. And his statement: 'known' – if its meaning is 'known in what is intended' then the common/mushtarak is excluded because its intended meaning is not known; if its meaning is 'known in clarity' then the common is not excluded from it. And it is excluded from his statement: 'individually' because its meaning then is that the meaning is separate from individuals and from another meaning, so both the common and the general are excluded from it. And he only mentioned the word/لفظ here rather than the arrangement/نظم following the principle, and because it is apparent that these divisions are not exclusive to the Book, rather they apply to all words of the Arabs. And he mentioned 'نظم' in the divisions out of observance of good style, because النظم originally means stringing pearls on a thread, unlike اللفظ which in language means throwing. As for mentioning the word 'كل' (every), even though it is objectionable in definitions in the terminology of logic, the purpose here is to explain inclusiveness and precision, and that is only achieved by the word 'كل'. The clarification: and it is either specificity of genus, or specificity of species, or specificity of individual. 'Known intended': i.e. known what is intended from it. (al-Qamar) 'because it etc.': i.e. because the common is placed for a meaning whose intended is not known. (al-Qamar) 'known in clarity' i.e. its clarity and manifestation from the word is known. (al-Qamar) 'individually' meaning that the word denotes a single meaning while disregarding the individuals; thus 'man' has no individuality because its individuals are considered. (al-Sanabali) 'because its meaning then etc.' He only said 'then' because the meaning of individuality is on the first estimation: the exclusion of the common from his statement 'known'; individuality from individuals excludes etc. because the common does not have individuality from another meaning, and the general does not have individuality from individuals; thus 'man' its individuals are considered. As for the dual, it is included in the specific because it includes two individuals, so it has disregarding of individuals (al-Qamar). 'not exclusive etc.' such that one is forced to produce (نظم) out of observance of good style. (al-Qamar) 'objectionable etc.' because 'كل' encompasses individuals, and definition is by essence not by individuals. (al-Qamar) 'to explain inclusiveness and precision' i.e. prevention of entry of others and encompassing all individuals of the defined. (al-Qamar) 'inclusiveness etc.' i.e. whenever the definition applies, the defined applies. (al-Sanabali) 'specifically of genus etc.' – if the word includes many that differ in legal rulings; or specifically of species: if it includes many that agree in the ruling; or specifically of individual: if it has a single meaning in reality. (Ifadat al-Anwar) Division of the specific after clarifying its definition: i.e. the specificity that is understood within the specific is either specificity of genus – that its genus is specific in meaning even if its referents are multiple; or specificity of species in the same manner; or specificity of individual i.e. the particular person – and this is the most specific. Genus with them [the jurists] is a universal predicated of many differing in purposes not in realities as the logicians hold; and species with them is a universal predicated of many agreeing in purposes not in realities as is the view of the logicians. They (the jurists) only investigate purposes not realities, so perhaps a species according to logicians is a genus according to jurists, as appears from the examples he mentioned like 'insan' (human): 'such as human, man, and Zaid'. So 'human' is analogous to specificity of genus, for it is predicated of many differing in purposes, for under it are man and woman; the purpose of creating man is his being a prophet, imam, witness in hadd and qisas punishments, performer of Friday and Eid prayers etc., and the purpose of woman is being a bed (for her husband), bringing children, managing household needs, etc. (al-Rada') [Note: The text mentions 'كالإرضاع' and 'كناكحا' but seems corrupt; likely intended as 'like the man is analogous to specificity of species, for it is predicated of many agreeing in purposes'] And 'man' (rajul) is analogous to specificity of species, for it is predicated of many agreeing in purposes. The phrase 'that its genus' – the correct is 'that it be a specific genus' etc. (al-Qamar) 'that its genus etc.': This is also a rebuttal of a presumed objection that genus being specific is inconceivable because genus implies multiplicity and specific implies singularity and individuality. The rebuttal: multiplicity and individuality are contradictory only if from one aspect, but here not so: multiplicity is in terms of referents and denotations, while unity and individuality are in terms of meaning and concept, so no contradiction. (al-Sanabali) 'in this manner' i.e. it is a species specific in meaning. (al-Qamar) 'i.e. the person etc.' interpretation of specific by specificity of individual. (al-Qamar) 'and this' i.e. the specific by specificity of individual. (al-Qamar) 'as held etc.' connected to the negative, and analogize to his subsequent statement 'as is the view of'. (al-Qamar) 'they (i.e. the usuliyun) investigate purposes because their goal is knowledge of rulings not realities.' (al-Qamar) 'he being a prophet': there is indication that prophethood is exclusive to men and no woman was a prophet; details in our gloss on Sharh al-Aqa'id titled 'Hall al-Ma'aaqid'. (al-Qamar) 'مستفرشة etc.' الاستفراش means the man's enjoyment of the woman. (al-Sanabali)
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of 'al-Khass' (the specific word) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by analyzing the wording of the definition: the word 'known' can be interpreted in two ways—either 'known in what is intended' which excludes mushtarak (homonym) because its intended meaning is unclear, or 'known in clarity' which does not exclude mushtarak. The phrase 'individually' ('ala al-infrad) ensures that the meaning is separate both from individuals and from another meaning, thereby excluding both the general and the homonym. The author then explains why the term 'lafz' (word) is used instead of 'nazm' (arrangement): it follows the standard principle and because these divisions apply to all Arabic words, not just Quranic text. However, in the subdivisions, 'nazm' is used for stylistic elegance. The use of 'kull' (every) in definitions, though unusual in logic, is necessary here to express comprehensiveness and precision. The main subdivision: the clarification (bayan) regarding the specific word is threefold: specificity of genus (jins), specificity of species (naw'), and specificity of individual ('ayn). The commentator elaborates: specificity of genus applies when the word includes many entities that differ in legal rulings (e.g., 'insan' includes man and woman who have different purposes/rulings). Specificity of species applies when the word includes many that share the same ruling (e.g., 'rajul' (man) where all individuals share the same legal purpose). Specificity of individual applies to a particular person (e.g., Zaid). The passage then contrasts the jurists' usage of genus and species with that of logicians: jurists focus on purposes (aghdar) related to legal rulings, while logicians focus on realities (haqa'iq). This explains why a species in logic (e.g., 'insan' is a species in logic but a genus in fiqh because it divides into man and woman with different rulings). The text also briefly discusses the dual (tathniya) as included in 'specific' because it covers two individuals while disregarding the rest. The commentator also addresses an objection about how genus can be specific—since genus implies multiplicity and specific implies singularity—by clarifying that multiplicity is in referents while specificity is in meaning/concept, so no contradiction.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khass): A word that indicates a single meaning individually, separate from other meanings and from individuals of a general category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the word 'المشترك' (homonym) excluded from being khass under the interpretation of 'ma'lum al-murad'?
A) Because it is a general word.
B) Because its intended meaning is not known.
C) Because it has multiple meanings but each is known.
D) Because it is not a word.

Key Takeaway
The definition of al-khass as 'a word indicating one meaning individually' excludes both homonyms and general terms through careful conditions.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
The Three Types of al-Khass: Jins, Naw', 'Ayn
Division of al-Khass into three types and comparison with logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of al-khass (jins, naw', 'ayn) and apply each to legal examples, while explaining the jurists' perspective on genus and species compared to logicians.

Core Idea
Al-Khass is threefold: by genus (different rulings), by species (same rulings), or by individual (single entity); jurists focus on legal purposes, not essences.

Now that we have a clear definition of al-khass, we turn to its threefold division: khass by genus (jins), by species (naw'), and by individual ('ayn). The passage also clarifies that jurists classify genus and species based on legal rulings (aghdar), differing from logicians who focus on essential realities. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly identifying which legal texts are specific in a given context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
From the passage: al-khass is either specificity of genus (khusus al-jins), specificity of species (khusus al-naw'), or specificity of individual (khusus al-'ayn).
These are the only three divisions.
2
Understand the difference between jins and naw'
Jins: the word includes many that differ in legal rulings (e.g., 'insan' includes man and woman with different rulings). Naw': the word includes many that agree in rulings (e.g., 'rajul' includes all men who share the same legal status).
The basis is the agreement or difference in legal purpose (ghard).
3
Apply examples given in the text
The text provides: 'insan' (human) as jins, 'rajul' (man) as naw', and 'Zaid' (proper name) as 'ayn. Verify each by noting the rulings under each category.
Men and women have different rulings in many areas (prophethood, testimony, etc.), so 'insan' is jins. All men share the same rulings (e.g., obligation of Friday prayer), so 'rajul' is naw'.
4
Compare jurists' view with logicians'
Jurists define genus/jins as a universal predicated of many differing in purposes (aghdar), not essences (haqa'iq). Logicians define genus as a universal predicated of many differing in essence. This leads to different classifications: e.g., 'insan' is a species in logic but a genus in fiqh.
The focus on purposes is because the jurists care about legal rulings, not metaphysical reality.
5
Note the inclusion of the dual
The dual (tathniya) is included under khass because it indicates two individuals and disregards the rest, meeting the condition of 'individuality' from general individuals.
This reinforces that 'individuality' means disregard of other individuals beyond those denoted.

مبحث الخاص

وقوله: "معلوم" إن كان معناه معلوم المراد فيخرج منه المشترك؛ لأنه غير معلوم المراد، وإن كان معناه معلوم البيان لم يخرج المشترك منه، ويخرج من قوله: "على الانفراد"؛ لأن معناه حينئذٍ أن يكون المعنى منفردًا عن الأفراد، وعن معنى آخر، فيخرج عنه المشترك والعام جميعًا، وإنما ذكر اللفظ ههنا دون النظم جريا على الأصل، ولأن الظاهر أن هذه الأقسام ليست مختصة بالكتاب، بل يجري في جميع كلمات العرب، وإنما ذكر النظم في التقسيمات رعاية للأدب؛ لأن النظم في الأصل جمع اللؤلؤ في السلك بخلاف اللفظ فإنه في اللغة الرمي، وأما ذكر كلمة "كل" فإنه وإن كان مستنكرًا في التعريفات في اصطلاح المنطق، ولكن القصد ههنا لبيان الاطراد والضبط، وهو إنما يحصل بلفظ " كل ".

The rebuttal: multiplicity and individuality are contradictory only if from one aspect, but here not so: multiplicity is in terms of referents and denotations, while unity and individuality are in terms of meaning and concept, so no contradiction. (al-Sanabali) 'in this manner' i.e. it is a species specific in meaning. (al-Qamar) 'i.e. the person etc.' interpretation of specific by specificity of individual. (al-Qamar) 'and this' i.e. the specific by specificity of individual. (al-Qamar) 'as held etc.' connected to the negative, and analogize to his subsequent statement 'as is the view of'. (al-Qamar) 'they (i.e. the usuliyun) investigate purposes because their goal is knowledge of rulings not realities.' (al-Qamar) 'he being a prophet': there is indication that prophethood is exclusive to men and no woman was a prophet; details in our gloss on Sharh al-Aqa'id titled 'Hall al-Ma'aaqid'. (al-Qamar) 'مستفرشة etc.' الاستفراش means the man's enjoyment of the woman. (al-Sanabali)
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of 'al-Khass' (the specific word) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author begins by analyzing the wording of the definition: the word 'known' can be interpreted in two ways—either 'known in what is intended' which excludes mushtarak (homonym) because its intended meaning is unclear, or 'known in clarity' which does not exclude mushtarak. The phrase 'individually' ('ala al-infrad) ensures that the meaning is separate both from individuals and from another meaning, thereby excluding both the general and the homonym. The author then explains why the term 'lafz' (word) is used instead of 'nazm' (arrangement): it follows the standard principle and because these divisions apply to all Arabic words, not just Quranic text. However, in the subdivisions, 'nazm' is used for stylistic elegance. The use of 'kull' (every) in definitions, though unusual in logic, is necessary here to express comprehensiveness and precision. The main subdivision: the clarification (bayan) regarding the specific word is threefold: specificity of genus (jins), specificity of species (naw'), and specificity of individual ('ayn). The commentator elaborates: specificity of genus applies when the word includes many entities that differ in legal rulings (e.g., 'insan' includes man and woman who have different purposes/rulings). Specificity of species applies when the word includes many that share the same ruling (e.g., 'rajul' (man) where all individuals share the same legal purpose). Specificity of individual applies to a particular person (e.g., Zaid). The passage then contrasts the jurists' usage of genus and species with that of logicians: jurists focus on purposes (aghdar) related to legal rulings, while logicians focus on realities (haqa'iq). This explains why a species in logic (e.g., 'insan' is a species in logic but a genus in fiqh because it divides into man and woman with different rulings). The text also briefly discusses the dual (tathniya) as included in 'specific' because it covers two individuals while disregarding the rest. The commentator also addresses an objection about how genus can be specific—since genus implies multiplicity and specific implies singularity—by clarifying that multiplicity is in referents while specificity is in meaning/concept, so no contradiction.

Key Terms
خصوص الجنس (khusus al-jins): Specificity of genus: a word that includes many entities differing in legal rulings (e.g., 'insan' includes man and woman).
خصوص النوع (khusus al-naw'): Specificity of species: a word that includes many entities agreeing in legal rulings (e.g., 'rajul' refers to all men sharing the same legal status).
خصوص العين (khusus al-'ayn): Specificity of individual: a word indicating a particular person or thing (e.g., 'Zaid').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of al-khass is illustrated by the word 'إنسان' (human)?
A) خصوص الجنس (Specificity of genus)
B) خصوص النوع (Specificity of species)
C) خصوص العين (Specificity of individual)
D) It is not specific.

Key Takeaway
Al-Khass divides into three types based on whether the word's referents differ in rulings (jins), agree in rulings (naw'), or refer to a single entity ('ayn); jurists' classification is purpose-based, not essence-based.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
beginner
6 min
Definition and Division of al-Khass
Defining al-khass and its types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-khass and distinguish between khass al-ayn and khass al-jins.

Core Idea
Al-khass is a word placed for a specific meaning; it is definitive unless evidence shows common use.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding al-khass (the specific) is crucial for correctly interpreting legal texts. This lesson introduces the definition of al-khass and its two main types based on the seminal text Noor al-Anwar. You will learn to identify specific words that indicate a particular meaning with certainty, setting the foundation for distinguishing them from general (amm) terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'al-khass' in the passage and note that it is the subject of the definition.
The passage begins with 'مبحث الخاص' (Topic of the Specific).
2
Extract the definition from the passage
From the commentary, derive that al-khass is a word placed for a specific meaning that does not admit commonality except by multiple conventions.
The literal translation states 'a specific meaning' and 'does not admit of commonality except by multiple conventions.'
3
Note the example given
The passage uses 'Zayd' as an example of a specific individual (khass al-ayn). Recognize that Zayd is a proper noun referring to a single person.
The text says 'وزيد نظير خاص العين' (and Zayd is an example of a specific individual).
4
Recognize the implicit division into two types
The phrase 'يوضع لأكثر من واحد' (placed for more than one) hints at a second type: khass al-jins (specific class). Thus, khass is divided into khass al-ayn and khass al-jins.
This division is standard in Hanafi usul, though the term 'khass al-jins' is not explicitly stated here.
5
Understand the legal effect
In Hanafi usul, al-khass is considered definitive (qat'i) in its indication when used without evidence of common use. This means the word conveys its meaning with certainty.
This is derived from the principle that a word placed for a specific meaning does not require further specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص

بيان حكم الخاص فإن أفراد الرجال كلهم سواء في الغرض، وزيد نظير خاص العين، فإنه شخص معين لا يحتمل الشركة إلا بتعدد الأوضاع.

بيان حكم الخاص] بأن يوضع لأكثر من واحد
Topic of the Specific. Explanation of the ruling of the specific: For the individuals of men are all equal in purpose, and Zayd is an example of a specific individual, for he is a specific person who does not admit of commonality except by multiple conventions. Explanation of the ruling of the specific: That it is placed for more than one.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of al-khass in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It begins by stating the ruling of the specific: that it refers to a particular meaning without sharing. The example of Zayd illustrates a specific individual (khass al-ayn), which is a particular person not shared by others. The passage also hints at the classification: khass al-ayn (specific individual) and khass al-jins (specific genus).

Key Terms
خاص (khass): A word placed for a specific meaning, either an individual or a class, that does not admit of commonality except by multiple conventions.
خاص العين (khass al-ayn): A specific individual, e.g., Zayd, that is not shared by others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Khass al-jins
B) Khass al-ayn
C) Khass al-naw'
D) Khass al-sifah

Key Takeaway
Al-khass is a definitive term indicating a specific meaning; it is divided into khass al-ayn (individual) and khass al-jins (class).

The Certainty of Khass in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the certainty of khass's indication and the opposing view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a khass (specific term) indicates its meaning with certainty and distinguish this from the opposing view.

Core Idea
Khass indicates its meaning with evidential certainty, because the purpose of language is communication.

The author has defined khass and divided it; now he turns to its legal effect (hukm). This effect is that a khass encompasses its denotation definitively, cutting off any probability of other meanings that is based on evidence. The key dispute is whether the mere possibility of metaphorical usage (majaz) undermines this certainty. We will explore the Hanafi resolution through a teleological argument and a refined definition of 'certainty'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling on khass
The text states that khass must encompass its denotation definitively (qat'an) such that no probability arising from evidence remains. Note the phrase 'qat'an' and its implications.
The example given is 'Zayd is knowledgeable': both words are khass.
2
Analyze the teleological proof
The Hanafi proof is: the purpose of placing a word for a meaning is to indicate that meaning upon utterance. If this indication were not definitive, the placement would be futile. This is a rational (aqli) argument.
This is a qiyas based on the wisdom of language.
3
Consider the opposing argument
The Samarqand scholars and Shafi'is argue that the possibility of majaz prevents certainty. Understand why they think this.
4
Understand the Hanafi response: two kinds of certainty
The author distinguishes absolute certainty (negating all possibilities) from evidential certainty (negating only possibilities with evidence). The khass's certainty is evidential; majaz without a qarina (contextual clue) is not a probability based on evidence.
Thus the possibility of majaz does not harm the certainty.
5
Apply the concept to a fiqh example
Consider the command 'Aqimu al-salah' (Establish prayer). The word 'al-salah' is a khass. According to Hanafis, it indicates the obligation of the known prayer with certainty, so no additional evidence is needed. The opponent would say the possibility of metaphorical prayer (e.g., du'a) leaves uncertainty.
This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ المصنف له عن تعريف الخاص وتقسيمه شرع في بيان حكمه، فقال: وحكمه: أن يتناول المخصوص قطعا أي أثره المترتب عليه أن يتناول المخصوص الذي هو مدلوله قطعًا بحيث يقطع احتمال الغير، فإذا قلنا: زيد عالم فزيد خاص لا يحتمل غيره احتمالاً ناشيًا عن دليل، وعالم أيضًا خاص لم يحتمل غيره كذلك، فكل واحد من الكلمتين يتناول مدلوله قطعا، فثبتت من مجموع الكلام قطعية الحكم بعالم على زيد بهذه الواسطة. وقال مشايخ العراق والقاضي الإمام أبو زيد وفخر الإسلام، وشمس الأئمة وتابعوهم مستدلين بأن الغرض من وضع اللفظ الدلالة عند الإطلاق، وإلا لم يكن للوضع فائدة، وقال مشايخ سمرقند وأصحاب الشافعي : إنه لا يتناول المدلول قطعاً لاحتمال المجاز ، أقول: إن القطع يطلق على معنيين: نفي احتمال الغير مطلقا، ونفي احتمال الغير احتمالاً ناشيًا عن دليل، وهذا أعم من الأول، والمراد ههنا هذا المعنى الأعم، واحتمال المجاز بدون ظهور القرينة ليس احتمالاً ناشيًا عن دليل فلا يضر القطعية
When the author finished defining and dividing the specific, he proceeded to explain its ruling, saying: Its ruling is that it encompasses the specified definitively, i.e., its effect is that it encompasses its denotation definitively such that the possibility of other than it is cut off. So when we say 'Zayd is knowledgeable', 'Zayd' is specific and does not admit of other than him with a probability arising from evidence, and 'knowledgeable' is also specific and does not admit of other than it, so each of the two words encompasses its denotation definitively. The scholars of Iraq, including the Chief Judge Abu Zayd, Fakhr al-Islam, Shams al-A'imma and their followers, argued that the purpose of placing a word is indication upon utterance, otherwise there would be no benefit in laying it down. The scholars of Samarqand and the followers of al-Shafi'i said that it does not encompass its denotation definitively due to the possibility of metaphor. I say: al-qat' is used in two senses: negation of the probability of other absolutely, and negation of the probability of other with a probability arising from evidence. This second is more general, and here the more general meaning is intended. The probability of metaphor without the appearance of a qarina is not a probability arising from evidence, so it does not harm definitiveness.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage establishes the Hanafi position that the specific indicates its meaning with certainty. The reasoning is teleological: the purpose of linguistic conventions is to communicate meaning, so if a word did not indicate its meaning definitively when uttered, the act of placing it would be futile. The opposing school (Samarqand/Shafi'i) argues that the possibility of metaphorical usage (majaz) prevents certainty. The Hanafi response distinguishes two kinds of certainty: absolute certainty (which excludes all possibility, even metaphorical) and evidential certainty (which excludes possibilities that have a basis in evidence). The certainty intended here is the latter, because a metaphorical interpretation without evidence is not a valid possibility.

Key Terms
قطعية (qat'iyyah): Definiteness of indication such that no valid probability of other meaning exists.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage that is possible but without evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars, why does the specific indicate its meaning with certainty?
A) Because the possibility of metaphor is impossible
B) Because the purpose of linguistic placement is communication, otherwise it would be futile
C) Because the Quran commands it
D) Because of consensus

Key Takeaway
A khass indicates its meaning with evidential certainty because the purpose of language is to communicate, and metaphorical possibilities without contextual evidence do not undermine that certainty.

The Specific (Khass) Does Not Accept Explanatory Elaboration: The Branch of Tuma'ninah
Explaining that khass does not accept bayan al-tafsir and the branch on tuma'ninah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the types of bayan that the specific accepts and those it does not, and apply this principle to the dispute over tuma'ninah in prayer.

Core Idea
The specific is definitive; only confirming, modifying, or abrogating elaboration is accepted—not explanatory.

This lesson explores a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: that a specific text (khass) is inherently clear and thus does not admit explanatory elaboration. However, it does accept other forms of bayan. This principle has a direct practical application in the dispute over whether tuma'ninah (calmness) in ruku' and sujud is a required component of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of bayan
List the four types: bayan al-tafsir, al-taqrir, al-taghyeer, and al-tabdil. Understand their meanings.
2
Understand the Hanafi position on khass
The khass does not accept bayan al-tafsir because it is already clear (bayyin bi nafsihi). It does accept the other three types.
Contrast with mujmal which needs tafsir.
3
Grasp the rationale
Bayan al-tafsir would be tahsil al-hasil (obtaining the already obtained), which is logically pointless.
4
Apply to the branch of tuma'ninah
The commands for ruku' and sujud are khass. Adding tuma'ninah as a fard would be a form of bayan al-tafsir, which is impermissible.
Thus tuma'ninah is not fard in the Hanafi school.
5
Compare with the opposing view
The Shafi'i school holds that khass does accept bayan al-tafsir, so they consider tuma'ninah a fard. Note the thamarah al-khilaf.
Practical difference: Hanafi rules tuma'ninah as sunnah; Shafi'i as fard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن الأول لبيان المذهب، والثاني لنفي قول الخصم ولتمهيد التفريعات الآتية، أي لا يحتمل الخاص بيان التفسير؛ لكونه بينًا بنفسه فهو مقابل للمجمل حيث يحتاج إلى بيان المجمل وتفسيره، وأما بيان التقرير والتغيير فيحتمله الخاص؛ لأنه لا ينافي القطعية، فإن بيان التقرير يزيل الاحتمال الناشي بلا دليل فيكون محكمًا كما يقال: جاءني زيد، وبيان التغيير يحتمله كل كلام قطعيًا كان أو ظنيًا كما يقال: أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار، وهكذا بيان التبديل يحتمله الخاص أيضًا. التفريع الأول على حكم الخاص] فلا يجوز إلحاق التعديل بأمر الركوع والسجود على سبيل الفرض، شروع في تفريعات مختلف فيها بيننا وبين الشافعي له على ما ذكر من حكم الخاص يعني إذا كان الخاص لا يحتمل البيان؛ لكونه بينا بنفسه لا يجوز إلحاق تعديل الأركان، وهو الطمأنينة في الركوع والسجود، والقومة بعد الركوع، والجلسة بين السجدتين بأمر الركوع والسجود، لنفي قول الخصم فإنّه قال: إنه يحتمل البيان
But the first is to state the school, and the second is to refute the opponent's statement and to pave the way for the following branches. That is, the specific does not accept bayan al-tafsir because it is itself clear. It is the opposite of the ambiguous (mujmal) which needs elaboration and explanation. As for bayan al-taqrir and bayan al-taghyeer, the specific accepts them because they do not contradict definitiveness. Bayan al-taqrir removes the probability that arises without evidence, so it becomes definitive, like saying 'Zayd came to me.' Bayan al-taghyeer is accepted by every speech, whether definitive or probable, like saying 'You are divorced if you enter the house.' Similarly, bayan al-tabdil is also accepted by the specific. The first branch on the ruling of the specific: it is not permissible to append the modification of the pillars to the command of ruku' and sujud on the basis of obligation. This is a branch in which there is a dispute between us and al-Shafi'i, based on the previously mentioned ruling of the specific. That is, since the specific does not accept elaboration because it is itself clear, it is not permissible to append the modification of the pillars, which is calmness in ruku' and sujud, and standing after ruku', and sitting between the two prostrations, to the command of ruku' and sujud, to refute the opponent's statement who says that it does accept elaboration.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This lesson explains the important Hanafi principle that the specific does not accept explanatory elaboration because it is inherently clear. However, it does accept confirmatory, modifying, and abrogating forms of elaboration. The first branch applies this principle to the issue of tuma'ninah in prayer. The Hanafis argue that since the commands for ruku' and sujud are specific and clear, they do not admit additional requirements like tuma'ninah by mere elaboration. The Shafi'is, who hold that the specific can accept elaboration, argue that tuma'ninah is included. This difference stems from the usul disagreement on whether the specific indicates its meaning with certainty.

Key Terms
بيان التفسير (bayan al-tafsir): Elaboration that explains an ambiguous term.
بيان التقرير (bayan al-taqrir): Elaboration that confirms and removes any doubt.
طمأنينة (tuma'ninah): Calmness and stillness in prayer postures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of bayan does the specific NOT accept according to the Hanafis?
A) Bayan al-taqrir
B) Bayan al-taghyeer
C) Bayan al-tafsir
D) Bayan al-tabdil

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the specific text is definitive and does not accept explanatory elaboration, leading to the ruling that tuma'ninah is not obligatory in prayer.

The Specific (Khass) Does Not Admit Explanatory Bayan: The Tranquility Dispute
Usul analysis of khass and dispute on tranquility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that al-khass is qat'i and does not admit explanatory bayan to derive the ruling on tranquility in prayer, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A specific (khass) divine command is self-evident; a hadith cannot add new fard obligations to it.

This passage from Taftazani's al-Talwih refines the usul concept of al-khass (the specific) by examining its acceptance of different types of bayan (clarification). The key application is the dispute over whether tranquility (tamkinah) in bowing and prostration is fard. The Hanafi school argues that the Quranic command 'wa rka'u wa sujdu' is khass and therefore does not admit explanatory bayan, so the hadith cannot impose tranquility as fard. This lesson will walk you through the usul reasoning and the inter-madhhab debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul principle
Understand the Hanafi definition of al-khass: a word indicating a specific meaning without ambiguity, self-evident, and not requiring explanatory bayan.
Khass is opposed to mujmal (ambiguous).
2
Classify the Quranic verse
Examine 'wa rka'u wa sujdu' (22:77) and classify it as khass because it clearly denotes the literal actions of bowing and prostrating.
The verse is specific in its command.
3
Apply the principle to the dispute
Since the verse is khass and does not admit explanatory bayan, the hadith of the man who prayed badly cannot be used to add tranquility as a fard element. The hadith is instructive for perfection, not for obligation.
The Hanafi response: the hadith does not override the clear text.
4
Contrast the Shafi'i position
Note that al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf consider the hadith as a bayan that makes tranquility fard. They interpret the Prophet's negation 'you have not prayed' as nullification of the prayer's validity.
Thamarah: different rulings on prayer validity if tranquility is omitted.
5
Understand the types of bayan
Recognise that khass can admit reaffirming (taqrir), changing (taghyir), and abrogating (tabdil) bayan, but not explanatory (tafsir) bayan. This refines the usul framework.
This distinction is critical for the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث الخاص
٤١
التفريع الأول على حكم الخاص
ولكن الأول لبيان المذهب، والثاني لنفي قول الخصم ولتمهيد التفريعات الآتية، أي لا يحتمل الخاص بيان التفسير؛ لكونه بينًا بنفسه فهو مقابل للمجمل حيث يحتاج إلى
الخاص
بيان المجمل وتفسيره، وأما بيان التقرير والتغيير فيحتمله الخاص؛ لأنه لا ينافي القطعية، فإن بيان التقرير يزيل الاحتمال الناشي بلا دليل فيكون محكمًا كما يقال: جاءني زيد، وبيان التغيير يحتمله كل كلام قطعيًا كان أو ظنيًا كما يقال: أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار، وهكذا بيان التبديل يحتمله الخاص أيضًا.
فتعين المطلق بالقيد
التفريع الأول على حكم الخاص]
فلا يجوز إلحاق التعديل بأمر الركوع والسجود على سبيل الفرض، شروع في تفريعات مختلف فيها بيننا وبين الشافعي له على ما ذكر من حكم الخاص يعني إذا كان الخاص لا يحتمل البيان؛ لكونه بينا بنفسه لا يجوز إلحاق تعديل الأركان، وهو الطمأنينة في الركوع والسجود، والقومة بعد الركوع، والجلسة بين السجدتين بأمر الركوع والسجود،
لنفي قول الخصم فإنّه قال: إنه يحتمل البيان (القمر) التفريعات الآتية: أي الثلاثة الأول من التفريعات الآتية. (القمر) لكونه بينا إلخ فلو بين لزم إثبات الثابت وهو تحصيل الحاصل. (السنبلي) وأما بيان التقرير إلخ: اعلم أن بيان التقرير توكيد الكلام بما يقطع احتمال المجاز أو الخصوص نحو جاءني زيد نفسه، ونحو قوله تعالى: ﴿فَسَجَدَ الْمَلائِكَةُ كُلُّهُمْ أَجْمَعُونَ) (الحجر:۳۰)، وبيان التغيير هو ذكر ما يغير الحكم السابق، كالشرط أو الاستثناء وبيان التبديل هو النسخ فإنه تبديل في حقنا وبيان في حق صاحب الشرع؛ إذ هو بيان لمدة الحكم المطلق التي كانت معلومة عند الله تعالى إلا أنه أطلقه فصار ظاهره البقاء في حق البشر. يزيل الاحتمال إلخ وما في "مسير الدائر"، فإنه يزيل الاحتمال الناشي عن دليل انتهى فمن زلة القدم. (القمر) فيكون أي الخاص الذي عرض له بيان التقرير (القمر) كما يقال : أنت طالق إلخ: فإن الشرط المؤخر في الذكر بيان مغير لما قبله من التنجيز إلى التعليق؛ إذ لو لم يكن قوله : إن دخلت الدار يقع الطلاق في الحال، وبإتيان الشرط بعده صار معلقًا. (القمر) بأمر إلخ متعلق بإلحاق، وكذا قوله: على سبيل الفرض.(القمر)
والقومة إلخ بالجر معطوف على التعديل، وكذا قوله: والجلسة. (القمر)

٤٢
التفريع الأول من التفريعات السبعة الخاص
مبحث الخاص
وهو قوله تعالى : وارْكَعُوا وَاسْجُدُوا على سبيل الفرض كما ألحقه به أبو يوسف (الحج : ۷۷) والشافعي رحمها وبيانه أن الشافعي له يقول: تعديل الأركان في الركوع والسجود فرض؛ لحديث أعرابي خفف في الصلاة فقال له عليه : " قم فصل فإنك لم تصل" هكذا قاله ثلاثًا، ونحن نقول: إن قوله تعالى : وارْكَعُوا وَاسْجُدُ والله خاص وضع المعنى معلوم؛ (الحج : ۷۷) لأن الركوع هو الانحناء عن القيام والسجود هو وضع الجهة على الأرض، والخاص
النبي
لا يحتمل البيان حتى يقال : إن الحديث لحق بيانًا للنص المطلق،
عن
كما ألحقه به أبو يوسف محه إلخ : تحقيق المرام: أنه عند الطرفين تعديل الركوع والسجود واجب ليس بفرض، وهو الطمأنينة، وزوال الاضطراب أقله قدر تسبيحة والقومة بعد الركوع والجلسة بين السجدتين ليستا ركنين يفوت الصلاة بفوتهما بل هما سنتان وقيل ،واجبتان وعليه اعتماد الشيخ ابن الهمام والفرض في الركوع متعلق الانحناء، وفي السجود وضع الجبهة على الأرض مع وضع القدم والفرض بين السجدتين ليس إلا ما ينفصل به السجدة الثانية الأولى، وتكلموا في مقدار رفع الوجه عن الأرض، وفي الهداية: أن الأصح أنه إذا كان إلى السجود أقرب لا يجوز؛ لأنه يعد ساجدًا، وإن كان إلى الجلوس أقرب جاز؛ لأنه يعد جالسًا فيتحقق الثانية، وقال الإمام أبو يوسف الله : أن تعديل الركوع والسجود فرض والقومة والجلسة ركنان وهو مذهب الشافعي له ومن تبعه مستدلين بما رواه الشيخان عن أبي هريرة أن رجلاً دخل المسجد ورسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جالس في ناحية المسجد فصلى، ثم جاء فسلم عليه فقال له رسول الله : وعليك السلام ارجع فصل فإنك لم تصل"، فرجع فصلى، ثم جاء فسلم، فقال: " وعليك السلام ارجع فصل فإنك لم تصل"، فقال في الثالثة أو التي بعدها: علمني يا رسول الله، فقال: إذا "قمت إلى الصلاة فأسبغ الوضوء، ثم استقبل القبلة فكبر ، ثم اقرأ ما تيسر معك من القرآن ثم اركع حتى تطمئن راكعا، ثم ارفع حتى تستوي قائما، ثم اسجد حتى تطمئن ساجدًا، ثم ارفع حتى تطمئن جالسًا، ثم اسجد حتى تطمئن ساجدا، ثم ارفع حتى تستوي قائمًا، افعل ذلك في صلاتك كلها ، فهذا الحديث دال على أن تعديل الركوع والسجود فرض، والقومة
والجلسة ركنان، فإن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نفى الصلاة بفواتها. (القمر) ونحن نقول أي من جانب الطرفين. (القمر) تخريجه: أخرجه البخاري في باب وجوب القراءة للإمام والمأموم في الصلوات كلها في الحضر والسفر، رقم: ٧٢٤، و مسلم في باب وجوب قراءة الفاتحة في كل ركعة رقم ۳۹۷ ، والنسائي في باب فرض التكبيرة الأولى، رقم: ۸۸۷، وأبو داود في باب صلاة من لا يقيم صلبه في الركوع والسجود، رقم ٨٥٦ ، والترمذي في باب ما جاء في
وصف الصلاة، رقم: ۳۰۳ ، وابن ماجه في باب إتمام الصلاة، رقم: ۱۰٦۰ ، عن أبي هريرة بألفاظ مختلفة.
Chapter of the Specific (al-Khass). The first derivation on the ruling of the specific. But the first is to state the madhhab, and the second to refute the opponent's statement and to pave the way for the following derivations. That is, the specific does not admit explanatory bayan (clarification) because it is self-evident; it is the opposite of the ambiguous (mujmal), which requires clarification of the ambiguous and its explanation. As for reaffirming bayan (taqrir) and changing bayan (taghyir), the specific admits them because they do not contradict certainty. For reaffirming bayan removes the probability arising without evidence and thus becomes definitive, like saying: 'Zaid came to me.' Changing bayan is admitted by any speech, whether certain or probable, like saying: 'You are divorced if you enter the house.' Similarly, abrogating bayan (tabdil) is also admitted by the specific. So the absolute becomes determined by the restriction. The first derivation on the ruling of the specific: Therefore it is not permissible to attach the adjustment (ta'dil) to the command of bowing and prostrating as obligatory. This is a beginning of derivations in which there is a difference between us and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, based on what was mentioned of the ruling of the specific. That is, if the specific does not admit bayan because it is self-evident, then it is not permissible to attach the adjustment of the pillars, which is tranquility (tamkinah) in bowing and prostrating, and standing up after bowing, and sitting between the two prostrations, to the command of bowing and prostrating. To refute the opponent's statement, for he said that it admits bayan... (commentary continues). And it is His saying: 'And bow and prostrate' (Quran 22:77) as obligatory, as Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i attached it to it. And the explanation is that al-Shafi'i says: Adjustment of the pillars in bowing and prostrating is obligatory because of the hadith of the Bedouin who shortened in prayer, so the Prophet said to him: 'Get up and pray, for you have not prayed.' He said this three times. And we say: Indeed His saying: 'And bow and prostrate' is specific (khass) and its meaning is known, because bowing is the inclination from standing, and prostrating is placing the forehead on the ground. And the specific does not admit bayan so that it can be said that the hadith came as a bayan for the absolute text. As Abu Yusuf attached it to it... (detailed discussion of positions).
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Tawdih (Taftazani's al-Talwih) is a critical refinement on the concept of al-khass (the specific) and its application. The author, al-Taftazani, engages in a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding whether the command to bow and prostrate (Quran 22:77) includes tranquility (tamkinah) as an obligatory part. The Hanafi position, based on the usul principle that al-khass is self-evident and does not admit explanatory bayan, holds that tranquility, standing after bowing, and sitting between prostrations are not obligatory (fard) but rather sunna or wajib. Imam al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf (within Hanafi school) consider them obligatory, citing the hadith of the man who prayed improperly (the 'Prophet taught him'). The passage explains that the specific cannot be clarified by a separate text because it is already clear; thus, the hadith cannot impose a new obligation on the clear command. The authoritative Hanafi position is that the fard in bowing is the minimum inclination, and in prostration is placing the forehead on the ground. The tranquility is wajib but not fard. The commentary further distinguishes between types of bayan: reaffirming (taqrir), changing (taghyir), and abrogating (tabdil). The specific admits only the latter two, not explanatory bayan. The analysis includes direct criticism of al-Shafi'i's reasoning and a detailed discussion of the hadith's implication.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khass): A word that indicates a specific individual or a specified meaning without ambiguity; it is self-evident and does not admit explanatory bayan.
بيان التقرير (bayan al-taqrir): Reaffirming bayan: restating the same meaning with emphasis to remove doubt of metaphorical or restricted interpretation.
تعديل الأركان (ta'dil al-arkan): The adjustment or straightening of the pillars of prayer, specifically tranquility in bowing and prostration, standing after bowing, and sitting between prostrations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul principle discussed in this passage, why can the hadith of the man who prayed badly not make tranquility a fard element of prayer?
A) Because the hadith is not authentic.
B) Because the Quranic command is khass and does not admit explanatory bayan.
C) Because tranquility is not mentioned in any hadith.
D) Because the hadith only applies to the Bedouin, not to all Muslims.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a specific (khass) divine command is decisive (qat'i) and cannot be altered by a zanni hadith; tranquility in prayer is wajib, not fard.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Ta'dil al-Arkan: Fard or Wajib? A Usul Analysis
Dispute over whether ta'dil al-arkan is fard or wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a khass text is qat'i al-dalala to evaluate whether a khabar al-wahid can elevate a wajib to fard.

Core Idea
Because the Quranic command 'Bow and prostrate' is khass, it cannot be elaborated by a solitary hadith; thus ta'dil al-arkan remains wajib, not fard.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute: does the hadith of the Bedouin, which commands tranquility (tuma'nina) in bowing and prostration, make ta'dil al-arkan fard or merely wajib? The Hanafi school argues that the Quranic verse 'Bow and prostrate' is a khass (specific) text that is qat'i in its indication, and a khabar al-wahid cannot elevate a wajib to fard. We will unpack the usul principles and the practical consequences of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual category of the Quranic command
Recognize that 'Bow and prostrate' is a khass (specific) command that directly indicates the essential acts of bowing and prostration, without specifying the manner (e.g., tranquility).
Khass is definitive in Hanafi usul: it does not admit elaboration via bayan except by an equally qat'i text.
2
Classify the hadith as khabar al-wahid
The hadith of the Bedouin, though authentic, is transmitted via a single chain (ahad), making it zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalala.
Zanni texts cannot override qat'i texts in Hanafi usul.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle: khass is qat'i
Since the Quranic command is khass, it definitively prescribes only the core actions. The additional detail of tuma'nina is not part of the khass and can only be required via a zanni proof, which establishes wujub, not fard.
This principle prevents khabar al-wahid from altering a qat'i ruling.
4
Determine the legal consequence
Ta'dil al-arkan is wajib (obligatory but not essential); omitting it makes the prayer deficient (makruh) but valid. The qawma and jalsa are sunnah according to the majority, though some Hanafis consider them wajib.
The practical difference: Shafi'is require repetition if tuma'nina is omitted; Hanafis do not.

Source Text

التفريع الأول من التفريعات السبعة الخاص
مبحث الخاص

وهو قوله تعالى : وارْكَعُوا وَاسْجُدُوا على سبيل الفرض كما ألحقه به أبو يوسف (الحج : ۷۷) والشافعي رحمها وبيانه أن الشافعي له يقول: تعديل الأركان في الركوع والسجود فرض؛ لحديث أعرابي خفف في الصلاة فقال له عليه : " قم فصل فإنك لم تصل" هكذا قاله ثلاثًا، ونحن نقول: إن قوله تعالى : وارْكَعُوا وَاسْجُدُ والله خاص وضع المعنى معلوم؛ (الحج : ۷۷) لأن الركوع هو الانحناء عن القيام والسجود هو وضع الجهة على الأرض، والخاص

والجلسة ركنان، فإن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم نفى الصلاة بفواتها. (القمر) ونحن نقول أي من جانب الطرفين. (القمر) تخريجه: أخرجه البخاري في باب وجوب القراءة للإمام والمأموم في الصلوات كلها في الحضر والسفر، رقم: ٧٢٤، و مسلم في باب وجوب قراءة الفاتحة في كل ركعة رقم ۳۹۷ ، والنسائي في باب فرض التكبيرة الأولى، رقم: ۸۸۷، وأبو داود في باب صلاة من لا يقيم صلبه في الركوع والسجود، رقم ٨٥٦ ، والترمذي في باب ما جاء في
وصف الصلاة، رقم: ۳۰۳ ، وابن ماجه في باب إتمام الصلاة، رقم: ۱۰٦۰ ، عن أبي هريرة بألفاظ مختلفة.
The first subdivision of the seven subdivisions concerning al-khass. Section on al-khass. It is His (Most High) statement: 'Bow and prostrate' as obligatory (fard) as Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on them) attached to it. Explanation: al-Shafi'i says: Adjusting the pillars in bowing and prostration is fard due to the hadith of the Bedouin who lightened (made brief) the prayer, to whom the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Get up and pray, for you have not prayed.' He said this three times. We (Hanafis) say: His statement 'Bow and prostrate' is a specific (khass) wording whose meaning is known because bowing is bending from standing, and prostration is placing the forehead on the ground. Al-khass cannot accept explanation (bayan) such that it could be said that the hadith came as an explanation of an absolute text, as Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) attached it. Achievement of the aim: According to both parties, adjusting bowing and prostration is wajib, not fard; it is tranquility (tuma'nina) and removal of restlessness, the minimum of which is the measure of one tasbih. The standing after bowing and the sitting between the two prostrations are not pillars such that the prayer is invalidated by their omission; rather they are sunnah, and some said wajib. Upon this is the reliance of Shaykh Ibn al-Humam. The fard in bowing is the bending itself, and in prostration it is placing the forehead on the ground along with the foot. The fard between the two prostrations is only what separates the second prostration from the first. They discussed the amount of lifting the face from the ground. In al-Hidayah: The most correct is that if he is closer to prostration, it is not allowed because he is considered prostrating; if he is closer to sitting, it is allowed because he is considered sitting, thus the second (prostration) is realized. Imam Abu Yusuf said: Adjusting bowing and prostration is fard, and the standing and sitting are pillars. This is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i and those who follow him, adducing what the two Shaykhs narrated from Abu Hurayra that a man entered the mosque while the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was sitting in a corner of the mosque, prayed, then came and greeted him. The Messenger of Allah said: 'And upon you be peace. Go back and pray, for you have not prayed.' So he went back and prayed, then came and greeted. He said: 'And upon you be peace. Go back and pray, for you have not prayed.' He said in the third or following (time): 'Teach me, O Messenger of Allah.' He said: 'When you stand for prayer, perform wudu perfectly, then face the qibla and say takbir, then recite what you can of the Quran, then bow until you are at ease in bowing, then raise yourself until you stand straight, then prostrate until you are at ease in prostration, then raise yourself until you are at ease sitting, then prostrate until you are at ease in prostration, then raise yourself until you stand straight. Do that in all your prayers.' This hadith indicates that adjusting bowing and prostration is fard, and the standing and sitting are pillars, because the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) denied the prayer due to their omission. (Al-Qamar) And we say from both sides. (Al-Qamar) Its takhrij: Al-Bukhari in Bab Wujub al-Qira'a..., Muslim, Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah from Abu Hurayra with different wordings.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents the first subdivision concerning al-khass, specifically the dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the ruling on ta'dil al-arkan (adjusting the pillars) in bowing (ruk') and prostration (sujud). The Hanafi position, supported by Abu Hanifa and the majority of the Hanafi school (including Abu Yusuf in one view and later scholars like Ibn al-Humam), is that adjusting the pillars (i.e., achieving tranquility and stillness, the minimum being the duration of one tasbih) is wajib (obligatory but not fard). The essential fard in bowing is simply bending; in prostration, it is placing the forehead on the ground. The standing after bowing (qawma) and sitting between prostrations (jalsa) are sunnah, though some considered them wajib. Abu Yusuf and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that ta'dil al-arkan is fard and that the qawma and jalsa are pillars (arkan), based on the well-known hadith of the Bedouin (al-A'rabi) in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) instructed the man to repeat his prayer because he had not performed it properly, explicitly commanding tranquility in each posture. The Hanafi response argues that the Quranic command 'Bow and prostrate' (khass) specifies only the actions themselves, not the manner of adjustment; the hadith, being a khabar al-wahid, cannot abrogate the Qur'anic text, which is qat'i al-thubut. Thus, the additional detail of adjustment is not fard but wajib, established through a zanni (speculative) proof. The author notes that both sides agree that ta'dil is required, but differ on its legal classification. The passage also includes a brief discussion on the minimum lifting of the face between prostrations, citing al-Hidayah. The hadith is then provided with its sources.

Key Terms
خاص (khass): A specific textual term that does not admit of elaboration (bayan) by a separate text unless it is abrogating.
تعديل الأركان (ta'dil al-arkan): Performing the pillars of prayer with tranquility (tuma'nina) and correct posture; the minimum is the time for one tasbih.
القومة (al-qawma): The standing upright after bowing.
الجلسة (al-jalsa): The sitting between the two prostrations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab (majority view), what is the ruling on ta'dil al-arkan (achieving tranquility) in bowing and prostration?
A) It is fard (essential).
B) It is wajib (obligatory).
C) It is sunnah mu'akkada.
D) It is mustahabb.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that ta'dil al-arkan is wajib, not fard, because the Quranic command is khass and a khabar al-wahid cannot elevate its ruling.

Khass Text and Wudu Conditions: A Hanafi Usul Analysis
Applying khass rule to wudu conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that a khass text cannot be elaborated by a khabar al-wahid to determine whether disputed conditions in wudu are fard.

Core Idea
The Quranic wudu verse is khass; ahad hadith cannot make its conditions fard.

This passage from Noor al-Anwaar applies a key Hanafi usul principle: a specific (khass) text like the wudu verse cannot accept additional conditions from a single-narrator hadith. This leads to the conclusion that continuity, sequence, intention, and basmala are not fard in wudu. We will examine the principle, its application, and the Hanafi response to opposing schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Usul Principle
State the rule: a khass text does not admit of elaboration (bayan) by a khabar al-wahid. Such elaboration would effectively be abrogation, which is not permissible because khabar al-wahid is zanni while the Quranic text is qati.
The passage begins by reminding us of this principle before applying it.
2
Apply the Principle to the Wudu Verse
The Quranic verse (5:6) commands washing, wiping, etc. It does not explicitly mention continuity, sequence, intention, or basmala. Any condition claimed from ahad hadith cannot be fard because the verse is khass.
This is the core application of the rule.
3
Examine the Conditions Claimed by Others
List the conditions: Malik claims continuity (tawali) as fard, Shafi'i claims sequence and intention, Zahiris claim basmala. Note their evidence: Malik relies on the Prophet's constancy (muwazaba), Shafi'i on the verse's order and hadith on intention, Zahiris on a hadith about basmala.
The passage summarizes the opposing positions and their proofs.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
For Malik: constancy does not prove obligation (e.g., i'tikaf is sunnah despite the Prophet's constancy). For Shafi'i: the verse's order is not necessarily fard; intention is proven by ahad hadith but makes it wajib only. For Zahiris: the basmala hadith is weak.
The Hanafi response uses both usul principles and hadith criticism.
5
Determine the Practical Difference
In Hanafi school, wudu without these conditions is valid (though makruh for omitting a sunnah). In other schools, it may be invalid (e.g., Zahiri for basmala, Shafi'i for deliberate disregard of sequence). This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
Understanding the practical consequence reinforces the importance of the usul principle.

=
Section on al-khass. 43. The second subdivision on the ruling of al-khass: It would only be abrogation, and abrogation by a single-narrator hadith is not permissible. Therefore, the status of each of the Book and the Sunnah must be considered. What is established by the Book is fard because it is qati; what is established by the Sunnah is wajib because it is zanni. The second subdivision on the ruling of al-khass: And the condition of continuity, sequence, naming (basmala), and intention in the verse of wudu becomes invalid. This is a second subdivision upon it, the ruling of al-khass. And it is attached to his statement 'It is not permissible' meaning: if al-khass does not admit of elaboration, then the condition of continuity becomes invalid, as Malik made it a condition. And the condition of sequence and intention as al-Shafi'i made them conditions. And the condition of naming as the Zahiris made it a condition in the verse of wudu, which is His statement: 'Wash your faces...' (al-Ma'idah: 6). Explanation: Malik says that continuity (al-wala') is fard in wudu, meaning that one washes his limbs in wudu consecutively and continuously such that the first limb does not dry (under normal air conditions), due to the Prophet's constancy upon it. And the Zahiris say that naming (tasmiya) is fard in wudu. 'It would only be etcetera' meaning: if the hadith is not an elaboration of the absolute text, then the hadith is only an abrogator of the absoluteness of the text, and it is a solitary hadith; abrogation by a solitary hadith is not permissible, for a solitary hadith is zanni and the text is qati, so we must act upon both. Thus, what is established by the Book (i.e., bowing and prostration) is fard, and what is established by the Sunnah (i.e., adjusting bowing and prostration, the standing and sitting) is wajib. Thus said al-Allama al-Halabi in Sharh al-Munya. (Al-Qamar) 'It is His statement etcetera': Allah Most High said: 'O you who believe, when you rise for prayer, wash your faces and your hands to the elbows, and wipe your heads and your feet to the ankles.' (Al-Ma'idah: 6) (Al-Qamar) 'Due to the Prophet's constancy': I say: Constancy does not indicate obligation. Do you not see that i'tikaf is a confirmed sunnah even though the Prophet (peace be upon him) was constant upon it? Rather, constancy is evidence of sunnah, as in al-Hidayah. Yes, his constancy upon it with disapproval of leaving it is evidence of obligation; ponder. (Al-Qamar) 'That tasmiya is fard etcetera': None of the four Imams went to the obligation of tasmiya in wudu except Imam Ahmad in the most authentic of two narrations from him. Ishaq said: If someone deliberately leaves tasmiya, he must repeat the wudu; if he forgets or interprets the hadith, it suffices. It was narrated from Dawud that he said: No wudu is valid except with it, whether he leaves it deliberately or forgetfully. Those who argue for obligation adduced the hadith narrated by al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah from Sa'id ibn Zayd: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: There is no wudu for one who does not mention the name of Allah upon it.' Ahmad and Abu Dawud narrated it from Abu Hurayra. The response: First, this hadith has been narrated through chains all of which are weak, as... (text cut off).
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the second subdivision concerning the ruling of al-khass. The principle established earlier is that a khass text (like the Quranic verse commanding wudu actions) does not admit of elaboration (bayan) by a khabar al-wahid. Therefore, any condition claimed to be part of the verse based on ahad hadith—such as continuity (tawali/ al-wala'), sequence (tartib), intention (niyya), or naming (tasmiya/basmala)—cannot be considered fard (essential). The author mentions that Malik made continuity a condition (such that limbs are washed consecutively without the previous limb drying under normal conditions), al-Shafi'i made sequence and intention conditions, and the Zahiris (apparent school) made basmala a condition. The Hanafi position rejects these as fard because they are not in the Quranic text and are only supported by zanni hadith, which cannot make something fard. The passage also includes a brief discussion on the evidence for these conditions: Malik's reliance on the Prophet's constant practice (muwazaba) is rebutted by the fact that constant practice does not prove obligation (e.g., i'tikaf is sunnah despite the Prophet's constancy). The only exception is if the constancy is accompanied by disapproval of leaving it. Finally, the specific issue of basmala in wudu is examined: the four Imams do not consider it fard (except Ahmad in one narration). The hadith cited by the Zahiris is weak. The text cuts off while discussing the response to the hadith.

Key Terms
الولاء (al-wala'): Continuity in wudu: washing limbs consecutively without the first drying under normal conditions.
التسمية (al-tasmiya): Mentioning the name of Allah (basmala) at the start of wudu.
الترتيب (al-tartib): Performing the actions of wudu in the order mentioned in the verse: face, hands, head, feet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view expressed in this passage, why are conditions like continuity (tawali) and basmala not fard in wudu?
A) Because the Quranic verse is khass and cannot be elaborated by ahad hadith.
B) Because there is no evidence for them at all.
C) Because the Prophet never practiced them.
D) Because they are impossible to fulfill.

Key Takeaway
A khass text from the Quran cannot be burdened with fard conditions from ahad hadith; such conditions are at most sunnah or wajib.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Tartib and Niyya in Wudu: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi refutation of Shafi'i claim that tartib and niyya are fard in wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi usul principles (al-khas, naskh by ahad reports) and hadith criticism to evaluate whether intention and order are obligatory (fard) in wudu, and distinguish between fard and wajib.

Core Idea
Intention and order are sunna, not fard, because the Quranic commands are self-evident (khas) and adding conditions via weak hadith would be abrogation.

This passage presents the Hanafi school's rigorous textual analysis against the Shafi'i claim that intention (niyya) and order (tartib) are obligatory (fard) in wudu. The Hanafis argue that the Quranic commands for wudu are limited to washing and wiping, and any additional conditions would require definitive proof, not weak hadith. They reinterpret the famous hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' as referring to reward, not validity, and cite prophetic practice to show that order was not strictly observed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i claim and its proofs
The Shafi'i school argues that intention and order are fard in wudu, citing the hadith 'Allah does not accept prayer...' with 'thumma' for order, and the hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' for intention. Identify these claims in the text.
The Shafi'i proofs are presented at the start of the passage.
2
Analyze the Hanafi critique of the order hadith
The Hanafis declare that the hadith used for order is weak (da'if) according to al-Nawawi, al-Darimi, and Ibn Hajar. They also provide evidence from prophetic practice where order was not required (e.g., forgetting to wipe the head).
This step involves evaluating the source criticism in the text.
3
Analyze the Hanafi interpretation of the intention hadith
The Hanafis argue that the hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' refers to reward, not validity. They cite the story of Companions migrating for worldly reasons—their migration was valid but without reward. Thus, wudu without intention is valid but lacks reward.
This is a key usul principle: distinguishing between validity (sihha) and acceptance (qabul) or reward (thawab).
4
Apply the Hanafi usul principle: al-khas and naskh
The Hanafis argue that the Quranic commands for wudu (washing and wiping) are specific (khas) and self-evident. Adding conditions like intention and order would be abrogation (naskh) of the clear Quranic text, which cannot be done by solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad).
This is the core textual analysis: the Quranic text is unambiguous (khas) and cannot be altered by weak or solitary hadith.
5
Distinguish between fard and wajib in Hanafi usul
The text explains that fard is established by qat'i (definitive) proof and its denier becomes a disbeliever, while wajib is by zanni (speculative) proof. In wudu, there is no wajib by consensus; only washing and wiping are fard. Order and intention are sunna.
This distinction is crucial for understanding Hanafi legal theory.

Topic of the Specific: Second Subdivision on the Ruling of the Specific. * Because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'There is no wudu for one who does not mention Allah's name.' And al-Shafi'i says to him: Indeed, order (tartib) and intention (niyya) in wudu are obligatory (fard); because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Allah does not accept the prayer of a person until he places the purification in its places, washing his face then his hands... the hadith.' = It is explicitly stated in Fath al-Qadir, and al-Tirmidhi reported from Imam Ahmad that he said: I do not know in this chapter a hadith with a good chain. And secondly, it is contradicted by a hadith reported by al-Daraqutni from Abu Hurayrah, Ibn Mas'ud, and Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever performs wudu and mentions Allah's name, his entire body is purified; and whoever performs wudu and does not mention Allah's name, only the place of wudu is purified.' (al-Qamar) Indeed, tartib means observing the order mentioned in the Book of Allah. (al-Qamar) And niyya is in terminology the intention of obedience and drawing near to Allah. (al-Qamar) Because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Allah does not accept ... etc.' for the word 'thumma' indicates order. This hadith has been declared weak by al-Nawawi and he said: it is unknown; al-Darimi added: it is not authentic; and Ibn Hajar said: it has no basis. So said 'Ali al-Qari. And in our school, tartib is sunna. Al-Allama al-Halabi said: Abu Dawud narrated in his Sunan that he (peace be upon him) forgot to wipe his head in his wudu, and then after finishing he wiped it with the moisture of his palm. Al-Daraqutni narrated from Layth ibn Sa'd said: 'Uthman came to the seats and called for water for wudu. He rinsed his mouth and sniffed water, then washed his face three times and his feet three times, then wiped his head, then said: I saw the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) perform wudu like this. (al-Qamar) And his hand three times. It was narrated by Abu Dawud in the chapter on mentioning Allah's name over wudu, no. 101; and Ahmad in his Musnad no. 9408 from Abu Hurayrah; and al-Tirmidhi in the chapter on what has been narrated regarding mentioning Allah's name during wudu, no. 25 from Rabah ibn 'Abd al-Rahman; and Ibn Majah in the chapter on what has been narrated regarding mentioning Allah's name in wudu, no. 397; and al-Daraqutni in the chapter on mentioning Allah's name over wudu from Abu Sa'id. Al-Tirmidhi said: Ahmad ibn Hanbal said: I do not know in this chapter a hadith with a good chain. Muhammad ibn Isma'il said: The best thing in this chapter is the hadith of Rabah ibn 'Abd al-Rahman. This hadith was declared weak by al-Nawawi and said: it is unknown; al-Darimi said: it is not authentic; Ibn Hajar said: it has no basis. As in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Manar by Mulla 'Ali al-Qari (Ishraq al-Absar 4). And al-Nasa'i narrated in his Sunan no. 1136, chapter on permission to omit remembrance in prostration; and Abu Dawud no. 858, chapter on the prayer of one who does not straighten his spine in bowing and prostration; and al-Daraqutni no. 4, chapter on the obligation of washing the feet and heels from Rifa'ah ibn Rafi' (may Allah be pleased with him) with this wording: 'The prayer of none of you is complete until he performs wudu perfectly as Allah Most High commanded him; he washes his face and hands up to the elbows; and wipes his head and feet up to the ankles; then he magnifies Allah Most High and praises Him and glorifies Him.' And because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Actions are but by intentions' and wudu is also an action, so it is not valid without intention. And we say: Indeed, Allah Most High commanded us in wudu with washing (ghusl) and wiping (mash), and these two are specific (khas) placed for a known meaning, which is pouring (isalah) and touching (isabah). So stipulating these things as the opponents have stipulated does not constitute an explanation (bayan) of the specific, because it is evident in itself, so it is nothing but abrogation (naskh), and it is not valid through solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad). At most, one should consider the status of each of the Book and the Sunnah: what is established by the Book is fard, and what is established by the Sunnah ought to be wajib. And it (the obligation in wudu) is washing and wiping. As in prayer, but there is no wajib in wudu by consensus, because the wajib is like the fard in terms of action. And because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Actions are but by intentions' - its meaning is that the validity of actions is by intentions. And we say: This hadith was narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim, and its story is that some of the Companions did not migrate for Allah but for marriage or trade, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Actions are but by intentions, and each man has only what he intended.' And the Prophet did not command them to renew the migration, even though migration at that time was fard 'ayn. So it is known that their migration was valid but reward was not attached to it. So the meaning of the hadith is: Indeed, the reward of actions is by intentions. So if one performs wudu without sincerity (or intention?), no reward attaches to it, but it is valid as a key to prayer. Then know that by 'actions' in the hadith is meant acts of worship, for many permissible acts are considered legally without intention, such as divorce and marriage. Thus said Ibn al-Humam. (al-Qamar) And it (the specific) is pouring and touching, i.e., general whether with continuity, order, naming, and intention or without them. Al-Allama al-Halabi said: Washing (ghusl) is pouring, and wiping (mash) in language is passing something over by way of touching; and in sacred law: applying the wet hand to what one is commanded to wipe. (al-Qamar) Except: abrogation, i.e., his saying with solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad) should not escape you that the hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' is a well-known (mashhur) report, as declared by al-Sayyid al-Sharif in his treatise on usul al-hadith. Indeed, the Umma has received it with acceptance in the first generation, and the Commander of the Faithful 'Umar said it in his sermon on the pulpit, and before him the Companions, and it is narrated in the authentic collections and Sunan with authentic chains. (al-Qamar) Like the fard: just as the doer of fard is rewarded, and the abandoner of fard deserves punishment, so too is the ruling for the doer of wajib and its abandoner. (al-Qamar) In terms of action, i.e., not in terms of belief, for the denier of fard is disbeliever, unlike the denier of wajib, because the fard is established by a definitive proof (qat'i), and the wajib by a speculative proof (zanni). (al-Qamar) It was narrated by al-Bukhari no. 1, chapter on how the revelation began for the Messenger of Allah; and Muslim no. 1907, chapter on his saying: 'Actions are but by intention'; and al-Tirmidhi no. 1647, chapter on what has been narrated regarding one who fights for show and worldly gain; and al-Nasa'i no. 75, chapter on intention in wudu; and Abu Dawud no. 2201, chapter on what is meant by divorce and intentions; and Ibn Majah no. 4227, chapter on intention, from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
The passage is a textual analysis and dispute concerning the Hanafi position that intention (niyya) and order (tartib) are not obligatory (fard) in wudu, contrary to the Shafi'i school. The author begins by citing the Shafi'i proof: the hadith 'La wudu' li-man lam yusamm' (no wudu for one who does not mention Allah's name) and the argument that order and intention are fard based on the hadith 'Allah does not accept prayer until one places purification in its places...' using the word 'thumma' for order. The author immediately counters by noting that this hadith is weak (da'if) according to major hadith scholars like al-Nawawi, al-Darimi, and Ibn Hajar. Then the author provides Hanafi evidence: the hadith from Abu Hurayrah and others about the comprehensive purification when mentioning Allah's name, and the practice of 'Uthman and the Prophet himself where order was not strictly observed (forgetting to wipe the head and later wiping it). The Hanafi position is that tartib is sunna, not fard. The second part deals with the Shafi'i invocation of the hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' (innama al-a'mal bi-nniyyat). The author explains that this hadith, despite being famous (mashhur), refers to reward (thawab), not validity (sihha). The proof is the story of the Companions who migrated for worldly reasons—their migration was valid but without reward. Similarly, wudu is valid without niyya, but reward depends on it. The author then makes a key usul point: the Quranic command to wash and wipe (ghusl and mash) is specific (khas) and self-evident; adding extra conditions like niyya and tartib would constitute abrogation (naskh), which cannot be done by solitary reports (akhbar al-ahad). The distinction between fard and wajib is also explained: both are binding in practice, but fard is established by qat'i proof and wajib by zanni proof. In wudu, there is no wajib according to the Hanafi school; everything beyond the fard acts is sunna. The passage concludes with the chain of the famous hadith on intentions.

Key Terms
الخاص (al-khāṣṣ): The specific (al-khas) refers to the explicit Quranic commands of washing and wiping in wudu, which are self-evident and do not require additional conditions like order or intention because their meaning (pouring and touching) is already clear.
النية (niyya): Intention: the intention of obedience and drawing near to Allah, which according to Hanafis is sunna in wudu (not fard) for validity, though necessary for reward.
الترتيب (tartīb): Order: performing the acts of wudu in the sequence mentioned in the Quran. In Hanafi, it is sunna, not fard.
الفرض (farḍ): An obligation established by a definitive (qat'i) proof; denial of fard leads to disbelief. In wudu, only washing and wiping are fard.
الواجب (wājib): An obligation established by a speculative (zanni) proof; denial does not lead to disbelief, but it is binding in practice. In wudu, there is no wajib by consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis consider order (tartib) in wudu to be sunna and not fard?
A) Because the Quran commands order explicitly and it is sunna to follow it.
B) Because the hadith used to prove order is weak, and prophetic practice shows order was not always observed.
C) Because the Quran mentions order with the word 'thumma' but it is not binding.
D) Because the Sahibayn differed from Abu Hanifa on this issue.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds that intention and order in wudu are sunna, not fard, grounded in the specificity of Quranic text (al-khas) and the principle that adding conditions requires definitive proof.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Al-Khass and Wudu: Intention as Sunnah
Applying usul principle of al-Khass to wudu intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that al-khass is self-evident to derive that intention in wudu is sunnah, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith 'innama al-a'mal bi-niyyat'.

Core Idea
A specific command cannot be elaborated by conditions; requiring intention in wudu would be abrogation, not elucidation.

This lesson examines how the Hanafi usul principle of al-khass is applied to the issue of intention (niyyah) in wudu. The passage argues that the Quranic commands to wash and wipe are specific and self-evident; therefore, making intention a condition would change the ruling, which is only permissible through abrogation (naskh)—and abrogation cannot be established by solitary hadith reports. The Hanafi school thus holds that intention in wudu is sunnah, not wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific texts
Locate the Quranic commands for wudu: washing the face and hands, and wiping the head and feet. These are al-khass, their meanings are clear (pouring and touching).
The commands are self-evident and do not require further elaboration.
2
Analyze the opponent's claim
The opponent (Shafi'i) uses the hadith 'innama al-a'mal bi-niyyat' to argue that intention is required for the validity of wudu.
The hadith is a solitary report (khabar wahid) according to some categorizations, but widely transmitted.
3
Apply the rule of al-khass
Since the original command is clear and specific, adding a condition would constitute abrogation (naskh), which cannot be done by a solitary hadith.
In Hanafi usul, abrogation requires definitive proof (qat'i al-thubut).
4
Determine the Hanafi alternative
The hadith is interpreted as referring to reward (thawab), not legal validity. The context of the emigration story supports this. Thus, intention is sunnah for reward, not a condition for validity.
Wudu without intention is valid but unrewarded.
