على سبيل التنزل: إن الفعل للوجوب كالأمر؛ لأنه عليها شغل عن أربع صلاة يوم الخندق فقضاهن مرتبة وقال صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي، فجعل متابعة أفعاله لازمة لأمته، فأجاب عنه المصنف له بقوله.
والوجوب أستفيد بقوله علية: صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي لا بالفعل؛ إذ لو كان الفعل موجبًا لاتبعوه بمجرد رؤية الفعل ولم يحتاجوا إلى هذا القول أصلاً، وقال تارة على سبيل الترقي:
الله
على سبيل التنزل [أي دون مرتبة الفعل عن الأمر القولي] يوم الخندق هو غزوة الأحزاب حفر المهاجرون والأنصار فيها خندقًا حول المدينة، وإنما سميت غزوة الأحزاب لاجتماع جماعات الكفار لقتال النبي كذا في بعض شروح صحيح البخاري"، وما يفهم من تفسير "الجلالين" من أن غزوة الأحزاب غير يوم الخندق فزلة عن القلم، وروى الترمذي عن عبد بن مسعود قال: إن المشركين شغلوا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن أربع صلوات يوم الخندق حتى ذهب من الليل ما شاء الله، فأمر بلالاً فأذن ثم أقام فصلى الظهر ، ثم أقام فصلى العصر ، ثم أقام فصلى المغرب، ثم أقام فصلى العشاء. (القمر) فأجاب عنه المصنف إلخ وقد أجاب ابن الهمام بأن قوله علي : "صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي" ما وقع بعد قضاء الصلوات يوم الخندق بل في حادثة أخرى، والأمر في هذا القول ليس للوجوب، فإن صلاته كانت تشتمل على السنن والمندوبات ولا تجب مع جميعًا. (القمر) والوجوب إلخ أي وجوب الإتباع في الصلاة استفيد إلخ وقد تسامح ههنا صاحب "التنقيح" حيث قال: وإيجاب فعله عليها أستفيد من قوله "صلوا" انتهى، فإن القول بأن كون الفعل موجبًا مستفاد من هذا الحديث هو عين دعوى الخصم أي بعض أصحاب الشافعي عليه فالأحسن ما قال المصنف. (القمر) لا بالفعل إيماء إلى أن أصل الجواب منع كون الوجوب مستفادًا من الفعل وإن ذكره المصنف في صورة الدعوى، وحينئذٍ لا مجال للمنع على قول المصنف والوجوب استفيد إلخ؛ لأنه يجوز أن يكون مستفادًا من الفعل لا القول، وإنما هو ادعاء محض تدبر . (القمر) لا تبعوه لأمر أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول (القمر) إلى هذا القول: أي صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي (القمر) على سبيل الترفي: [أي على سبيل استواء الفعل مع القول]
عبد
أخرجه الترمذي في "جامعه رقم: ۱۷۹، باب ما جاء في الرجل تفوته الصلوات بأيتهن تبدأ، قال الترمذي: حديث الله ليس بإسناده بأس، والنسائي رقم: ٦٦٢ ، باب الاجتزاء لذلك كله بأذان واحد والإقامة لكل واحدة منهما، وأحمد في "مسنده"، رقم: ٣٥٥٥ ، عن عبد الله بن مسعود ، وليس فيهما لفظ: "صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي".
The Topic of the Specific. Definition of the Command. In a descending manner: That action indicates obligation like the verbal command, because he (the Prophet) was occupied from four prayers on the day of the trench, so he performed them in order and said: 'Pray as you see me praying.' So he made following his actions obligatory upon his community. The author responded to him with his saying: 'And obligation is derived from his saying, peace be upon him: "Pray as you see me praying," not from the action; for if the action were obligatory, they would have followed it merely by seeing the action and would not have needed this saying at all.' And he said sometimes in an ascending manner: ... 'In a descending manner [i.e., below the rank of action from the verbal command]. The day of the trench is the Battle of the Confederates. The emigrants and helpers dug a trench around Medina, and it was called the Battle of the Confederates due to the gathering of groups of disbelievers to fight the Prophet, as mentioned in some commentaries of Sahih al-Bukhari. What is understood from Tafsir al-Jalalayn that the Battle of the Confederates is not the day of the trench is a slip of the pen. Al-Tirmidhi narrated from Ibn Mas'ud: 'Indeed the polytheists occupied the Messenger of Allah from four prayers on the day of the trench until such time of the night as Allah willed. So he ordered Bilal to call the adhan, then iqamah, and he prayed Zuhr; then iqamah and he prayed Asr; then iqamah and he prayed Maghrib; then iqamah and he prayed Isha.' (al-Qamar). So the author responded... And Ibn al-Humam responded that his saying 'Pray as you see me praying' did not occur after the performance of the prayers on the day of the trench, but in another incident, and the command in this saying is not for obligation, for his prayer included sunan and mustahabbat, and they are not all obligatory (al-Qamar). And 'the obligation, etc.' meaning the obligation of following in prayer is derived... And the author of al-Tanqih was lenient here where he said: 'And the obligation of his action upon them is derived from his saying "Pray."' For the statement that the action being obligatory is derived from this hadith is exactly the claim of the opponent i.e., some of the Shafi'is. So what the author said is better (al-Qamar). 'Not from the action' hints that the basis of the response is prevention that obligation is derived from the action, even if the author mentioned it in the form of a claim. So then there is no room for prevention according to the author's statement 'and obligation is derived, etc.' because it is possible that it is derived from the action not the saying, and it is mere claim. Contemplate (al-Qamar). ... 'to this saying': i.e., 'Pray as you see me praying' (al-Qamar). In an ascending manner: [i.e., on the level of equality of action with the saying]. Narrated by al-Tirmidhi in his Jami', no. 179, chapter on the man who misses prayers and with which to start. Al-Tirmidhi said: the hadith has no problem in its chain. And al-Nasa'i no. 662, chapter on being satisfied with one adhan for all and iqamah for each. And Ahmad in his Musnad, no. 3555, from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, and in both there is not the wording 'Pray as you see me praying'.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on the matn, defines the concept of 'amr' (command) and its types: verbal command (amr qawli) and action command (amr fi'li). The author presents a descending approach (tanazzul) where action is said to indicate obligation like the verbal command, citing the incident of the Battle of the Trench (Khandaq). The Prophet was prevented from four prayers and later made them up in order, and said 'Pray as you see me praying.' This seems to imply that his action itself indicates obligation. However, the commentator (the musannif) responds that the obligation is derived from the Prophet's saying, not from the action itself. He argues that if the action alone were sufficient, the Companions would have followed immediately without needing the verbal command. Then he presents an ascending approach (taraqqi) where action is considered equal to verbal command. The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud is cited from al-Tirmidhi as evidence. Ibn al-Humam further clarifies that the hadith in question (Pray as you see me) actually occurred in a different incident, not after the Trench prayers, and that the command in that saying is not for obligation because the Prophet's actions include recommended elements. The author of al-Tanqih mistakenly thought the obligation from the action was derived from the hadith, but the commentator corrects this. The opponent here is some Shafi'i scholars who claim that the Prophet's action itself indicates obligation. The Hanafi position, as defended here, is that the Prophet's action does not by itself obligate; rather, it must be accompanied by a verbal command or a clear indication of obligation. This is a key usul principle distinguishing Hanafi methodology.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The command; in this passage it is divided into two types: verbal (قولي) and action (فعلي).
التنزل (al-tanazzul): The descending approach, where the action is placed at a lower rank than the verbal command in indicating obligation.
الترقي (al-taraqqi): The ascending approach, where the action is given equal rank to the verbal command in indicating obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, from what is the obligation to follow the Prophet's prayer derived?
A) From the Prophet's action itself, because it is a type of command.
B) From the Prophet's saying 'Pray as you see me praying'.
C) From the consensus of the Companions who prayed after him.
D) From the Quranic verse 'And obey Allah and the Messenger'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between verbal command and action: obligation is derived from the verbal command, not from the action alone.

The Command Necessitates Obligation: Hanafi Position
Explaining that the command's necessitator is obligation, not recommendation, permission, or suspension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the default ruling of a command and identify the opposing views on tawaqquf.

Core Idea
The command (amr) by itself indicates obligation (wujub); any other meaning requires a contextual indication.

This lesson explains the foundational Hanafi principle that the command (amr) in the Shariah primarily indicates obligation. We will examine the supporting arguments and the opposition from some Shafi'i scholars who propose suspension (tawaqquf). Understanding this principle is essential for deriving rulings from Quranic and Sunnah commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The passage states that the necessitator (mujib) of the command is obligation (wujub) alone, not recommendation (nadb), permission (ibaha), or suspension (tawaqquf).
The term 'mujib' here means the legal effect established by the command.
2
Define the terms
Obligation: doing the act brings reward, omission brings punishment. Recommendation: doing it brings reward, omission has no punishment. Permission: both doing and omitting are equal.
These definitions are standard across schools.
3
Examine the opposing view: tawaqquf
Some Shafi'i scholars (Ibn Shurayh) hold that 'amr' is homonymous between obligation, recommendation, and permission, so when no qarīna is present, one must pause (tawaqquf) to determine the intended meaning.
Al-Ghazali and others also discuss this but differ on the basis.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response is that the default in language is literal (haqiqa), and the literal meaning of 'amr' is obligation. Any deviation requires a qarīna; homonymy would create unwarranted ambiguity in revelation.
The passage emphasizes that most scholars (jumhur) agree with the Hanafi view.
5
Apply the principle
When encountering a command in the Quran or Sunnah without a qarīna, the Hanafi ruling is that the commanded act is obligatory. This has direct implications for deriving fiqh rulings.
Example: 'Establish prayer' (Baqara 2:43) indicates that prayer is obligatory.

The Topic of the Specific. 73. Explanation of the Necessitator of the Command. Indeed the action is a part of the command, because the command is of two types: statement and action. Because Allah the Exalted applied the word 'amr' to action in His saying: 'And the command (amr) of Pharaoh is not right-minded' (Hud 11:97) i.e., his action; because a statement is not described as 'right-minded'; it is only described as 'sound'. So the author responded to him with his saying: 'And the action is called by it because it is its cause.' i.e., the action is called by the word 'amr' because the command is a cause for the action, so it falls under metaphor (majaz). But the discussion is about the literal (haqiqa). [Explanation of the Necessitator of the Command] i.e., for its obligation. And when he finished from refuting synonymy (taraduf) intentionally, he began refuting homonymy (ishtirak) intentionally, so he said: And its necessitator (mujib) is obligation, not recommendation, permission, or suspension (tawaqquf). Meaning that the necessitator of the command is only obligation. 'It is a part of the command, etc.': Its formulation: That the action is a command, etc., and every command indicates obligation, so the action indicates obligation. But the major premise may be prevented: why is it not permissible that an individual of command (i.e., the statement) indicates obligation? (al-Qamar) 'Not described as right-minded, etc.' and similar to that is His saying: 'And their command (amr) is consultation among them' (Shura 42:38) i.e., their action. And His saying: 'And you disputed concerning the command (amr)' (Al Imran 3:152) i.e., concerning the action they were to undertake. And His saying: 'Do you wonder at the command (amr) of Allah?' (Hud 11:73) i.e., His doing. And the principle in unrestricted usage is the literal (haqiqa), and what has been applied to command in the literal sense indicates obligation without disagreement, so the action indicates obligation like the verbal formula. (al-Sanbali) So the author responded, etc.: This is a response after conceding that what is meant by 'amr' in the verse is action. But the basis of the response is prevention (man'): why is it not permissible that what is meant by 'amr' in the verse is 'matter' (sha'n) or 'affair' (tarb), or what is meant is the verbal command, indicated by the preceding verse: 'So they followed the command (amr) of Pharaoh' i.e., they obeyed him in what he commanded them, and 'The command (amr) of Pharaoh is not right-minded' (Hud 11:97). And then describing it as 'right-minded' is a case of describing the thing with the description of its possessor, like 'painful punishment' while the painful one is the punished. (al-Qamar) 'It is from the category of metaphor: by applying the name of the cause to the effect (musabbab).' (al-Qamar) 'And suspension, etc.': The intent of the one who says suspension is that it is fixed in determining the intended meaning in usage, not that the suspension is in determining the meaning (wad'). Because according to him, it is placed by homonymy (ishtirak lafzi) for obligation, recommendation, and permission. This is the saying of Ibn Shurayh of the Shafi'is. And al-Ghazali and a group of investigators said: The suspension is in determining the meaning (wad'), because it is placed for obligation only, or recommendation only, or homonymy between them. (al-Sanbali) 'The necessitator of the command, etc.': i.e., the established effect (athar) of the command is obligation according to most scholars. It is the permissibility of the act with the prohibition of its omission. Recommendation is the permissibility of the act with its superiority. Permission is the permissibility of the act with the permissibility of omission. Then know that 'al-mujib' (with fatha on jim) and 'al-muqtaḍā' and 'al-hukm' are synonymous terms according to the jurists, as mentioned in 'Mishkat al-Anwar'. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage explains the meaning of 'mujib al-amr' (the necessitator of the command). It begins by stating that action is a type of command because command is of two types: statement and action. The proof is from the Quranic verse 'And the command of Pharaoh is not right-minded' where 'amr' is interpreted as action. The author responds that this usage is metaphorical (majaz), as command is the cause of action, so the action is called command by metaphor. The main discussion is about the literal (haqiqa) meaning of the command. After refuting synonymy and homonymy, the author states that the necessitator of the command is obligation (wujub) exclusively, not recommendation (nadb), permission (ibaha), or suspension (tawaqquf). The commentary (from the musannif) then analyzes the argument. It points out that the major premise 'every command indicates obligation' can be challenged because only the verbal command type may indicate obligation. It also provides alternative interpretations of the verse to avoid the conclusion that action indicates obligation. The text discusses the difference between suspension in usage (tawaqquf fi al-isti'mal) and suspension in the meaning (tawaqquf fi al-wad'). According to some Shafi'is like Ibn Shurayh, the word 'amr' is homonymous between obligation, recommendation, and permission, so when used alone, one must pause to determine which is intended. Al-Ghazali and others say the word is not homonymous but has a single meaning, but there is disagreement on what that meaning is. The Hanafi position is clear: the command on its own indicates obligation unless there is a qarīna (indication) to the contrary. This is the relied-upon view. The passage concludes by defining the technical terms: obligation (wujub) is the demand to do an act with a binding commission, resulting in reward for compliance and punishment for omission; recommendation (nadb) is the demand to do an act without binding commission, resulting in reward for compliance but no punishment for omission; permission (ibaha) is the option to do or not do, with no reward or punishment. The terms 'mujib', 'muqtada', and 'hukm' are synonymous in fiqh context.

Key Terms
موجب الأمر (mujib al-amr): The effect (athar) established by the command: obligation (wujub). It is the legal consequence that the commanded act is binding.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The binding commission: doing the act is rewarded, omitting it is punished.
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspension or pause; in this context, it refers to the opinion that one must pause to determine the intended meaning of a command (obligation, recommendation, or permission) when no qarīna is present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the default ruling (hukm) of a command (amr) when no qarīna is present?
A) It indicates recommendation (nadb).
B) It indicates permission (ibaha).
C) It indicates obligation (wujub).
D) It is suspended (tawaqquf) until further evidence.

Key Takeaway
The command (amr) by itself indicates obligation (wujub) in the Hanafi school; this is the mufta bihi position.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

The Imperative Form: Obligation or Ambiguity?
Explaining the Hanafi position on the imperative against four opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the imperative form, identify the four main opposing views, apply the Hanafi response to a cited proof, and evaluate the practical consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
The imperative defaults to obligation; other meanings require a qarīna.

When the Quran or Sunnah commands an action, how do we know whether it is obligatory, recommended, or permissible? This is a foundational usul question. The Hanafi school holds that the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr) is literally for obligation (wujūb) in its absolute usage. This lesson unpacks the dispute between the Hanafis and other groups, and explains how the Hanafi school rebuts the arguments for recommendation, permission, suspension, and homonymy. Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly deriving rulings from primary texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four main opposing views
List the groups that disagree with the Hanafi position: those who say the imperative indicates recommendation (ahl al-nadb), permission (ahl al-ibāḥa), suspension (mutawaqqifūn), and those who claim homonymy (ishtirāk lafẓī or ma‘nawī).
The text also mentions a variant within the Mālikī school holding permission, and al-Shāfi‘ī’s reported homonymy.
2
Examine the Hanafi response to each opposing proof
For each opposing view, identify the proof they cite (e.g., verse on kitāba for nadb, verse on hunting for ibāḥa, sixteen usages for tawaqquf). Then explain how the Hanafis respond: those verses have qarīna indicating a non-obligatory meaning; the absolute imperative without qarīna defaults to obligation.
The key principle: the imperative is a request, and the complete request implies binding compliance.
3
Understand the Hanafi definition of wujūb
The text clarifies that wujūb here means linguistic bindingness (luzūm), not just juristic wujūb. Therefore it covers both definitively obligatory (qaṭ‘ī) and conjecturally obligatory (ẓannī) commands, such as those from solitary reports.
This wider scope is important for applying the rule to ahadith.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference in fiqh: a command like 'Give zakāt' without qualification is obligatory according to Hanafis, but a Shāfi‘ī who considers it a homonym may need to look for qarīna before concluding obligation, potentially treating it as recommended until evidence arises.
This shows that the dispute is not merely semantic; it directly affects fatwa.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text indicates that the Hanafi position is the chosen madhhab (al-mukhtār). The reasoning is that the imperative is a request, and the default in a request is that the requested action is binding. The sixteen meanings cited by the mutawaqqifūn are all usages with qarīna; they do not negate the literal meaning.
Tarjih here relies on linguistic default and the lack of evidence for ambiguity in the absolute form.

بيان موجب الأمر
عند العامة لا الندب كما ذهب إليه بعض ولا الإباحةُ كما ذهب إليه بعض، ولا التوقفُ كما ذهب إليه بعض، ولا الاشتراك لفظًا أو معنى بين الثلاثة أو الاثنين كما ذهب إليه آخرون، و لم يذكره المصنف؛ لأنه يفهم مما ذكره التزاما، فأهل الندب يقولون : الأمر للطلب فلابد أن يكون جانب الفعل فيه راجحًا حتى يطلب، وأدناه الندب، وهذا كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَكَاتِبُوهُمْ إِنْ عَلِمْتُمْ فِيهِمْ خَيْراً﴾ (النور: 33) وأهل الإباحة يقولون: إن معنى الطلب أن يكون مأذونا فيه، ولا يكون حراما، وأدناه هو الإباحة، كما ذهب إليه بعض هو أبو هاشم، وأكثر المعتزلة، ويروى عن الشافعي في قول. ولا الإباحة كما نقل عن بعض أصحاب مالك. وأهل التوقف ذهب إليه بعض هو أبو العباس أحمد بن شريح من أصحاب الشافعي، ثم اعلم أن التوقف عنده في تعين المراد عند الاستعمال كما يشعر عليه قول الشارح فيما سيأتي، فيجب التوقف لا في تعيين الموضوع له، فإن الأمر عنده موضوع بالاشتراك اللفظي للوجوب والندب والإباحة والتهديد. ولا الاشتراك لفظا إلخ اعلم :أولاً: أن الاشتراك اللفظي عبارة عن كون اللفظ موضوعا لكل واحد من المعاني ابتداء، والاشتراك المعنوي عبارة عن كون اللفظ موضوعًا لمعنى واحد كلي له أفراد. وثانيا: أنه روي عن الشافعي أنه مشترك لفظًا بين الوجوب والندب، ونقل عن الشيخ أبي منصور الماتريدي أنه موضوع للاقتضاء حتمًا كان أو ندبًا، فصار مشتركًا معنويًا بينهما، وقيل: هو مشترك لفظًا بين الوجوب والندب والإباحة، وقيل: مشترك معنى بين هذه الثلاثة بأن يكون موضوعًا للإذن الشامل لهذه الثلاثة وهو مذهب المرتضى من الشيعة. لأنه يفهم إلخ فإنه لما نفى كون الندب والإباحة موجب الأمر فهم أنه ليس مشتركا بين الاثنين أو الثلاثة، ولما قال: إن موجب الأمر الوحوب، فهم أنه ليس مشتركًا معنًا بين الثلاثة أو الاثنين، فإنه على الأول موجبه الإذن، وعلى الثاني موجبه الاقتضاء على ما مرّ آنفا تدبر. وأدناه الندب فإن في الإباحة الطرفان متساويان، وأما المنع عن الترك كما هو في الوجوب فأمر زائد على الرجحان. وأدناه الندب إلخ قلنا الأمر طلب، فمطلقه ينصرف إلى الكامل و ذا بالوجوب. فكاتبوهم إلخ : قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ يَبْتَغُونَ الْكِتَابَ مِمَّا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ فَكَاتِبُوهُمْ إِنْ عَلِمْتُمْ فِيهِمْ خَيْرا﴾ (النور: 33) أي إصلاحًا واستطاعة لأداء مال المكاتبة وهي أن يقول السيد لعبده كاتبتك على كذا من المال، فإن أداه عتق، وإن بقي عليه شيء فهو عبد. فأمر الكتابة ههنا للندب.

وهذا كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاصْطَادُوا﴾ (المائدة: 2) فالاصطياد مباح. والمتوقفون يقولون: إن الأمر يستعمل لستة عشر معنى كالوجوب، والإباحة والندب والتهديد والتعجيز والإرشاد، والتسخير وغير ذلك، فما لم تقم قرينة على أحدها لم يُعمل به، فيجب التوقف حتى يتعين المراد، وعندنا الوجوب حقيقة الأمر، فيحمل عليه مطلقه ما لم تقم قرينة خلافه، وإذا قامت قرينته يحمل عليه على حسب المقام. سواء كان بعد الحظر أو قبله متعلق بقوله : وموجبه الوجوب، وردُّ على من قال : إن الأمر بعد الحظر للإباحة، وقبله للوجوب على حسب ما يقتضيه العقل والعادة. لستة عشر معنى: (1) الوجوب نحو أَقِيمُوا الصَّلاةَ (الأنعام: 72) (2) الإباحة كما مرّ آنفًا (3) الندب كما سبق، (4) التهديد، وهو مخاطبة الغير بالغضب نحو: اعْمَلُوا مَا شِتُمْ (فصلت: 40) ويقرب منه الإنذار، وإن جعلوه قسما آخر وهو إبلاغ مع تخويف نحو: وقُلْ تَمَتَّعْ بِكُفْرِكَ قَلِيلا (الزمر: 8) (5) التعجيز نحو: فَأْتُوا بِسُورَةٍ مِّنْ مِثْلِهِ (البقرة: 23) (6) والإرشاد نحو: وَأَشْهِدُوا ذَوَيْ عَدْلٍ مِنْكُمْ (الطلاق: 2) وهو قريب من الندب إلا أنه يتعلق بالمصالح الدنيوية، والندب لثواب الآخرة. (7) التسخير نحو: كُونُوا قِرَدَةً خَاسِئِينَ (البقرة: 65)، (8) الامتنان نحو: وَكُلُوا مِمَّا رَزَقَكُمُ اللَّهُ (الأنعام: 142)، فإن هذا الأمر للامتنان بقرينة قوله: وَكُلُوا مِمَّا رَزَقَكُمُ اللَّهُ (الأنعام: 142)، (9) الإكرام نحو: قوله تعالى: ادْخُلُوهَا بِسَلامِ آمِنِينَ (الحجر: 46)، فإن السياق قرينة على أن الأمر للإكرام، (10) الإهانة كما تقول لمن تهمينه ذق، (11) التسوية كقوله تعالى: فَاصْبِرُوا أَوْ لَا تَصْبِرُوا (الطور: 16) (12) الدعاء نحو: اللهم اغفر لي، (13) التمني نحو: يَا مَالِكُ لِيَقْضِ عَلَيْنَا رَبُّكَ (الزخرف: 77)، (14) الاحتقار نحو: قول موسى عليه السلام للسحرة احتقارًا لهم: أَلْقُوا مَا أَنْتُمْ مُلْقُونَ (يونس: 80)، (15) التكوين نحو كن (16) التأديب نحو: قوله عليه السلام لابن عباس: "كل" مما يليك، وهو قريب من الندب إلا أن الندب لثواب الآخرة، والتأديب لتهذيب الأخلاق وإصلاح العادات. الوجوب حقيقة الأمر إلخ المراد بالوجوب اللزوم، والوجوب اللغوي لا الفقهي، فيشمل الواجب القطعي والظني؛ لأن من أفراد الأمر ما ثبت بخبر الواحد وهو ظني ولو خصّ بالأمر القرآني لكان معناه اللزوم القطعي لا ما تعمهما كذا في "مشكاة الأنوار". سواء كان بعد الحظر إلخ بيان للمذهب المختار عندنا، وهو للوجوب بعد المنع أو قبله للدلائل السابقة، فإنها لا تفرق بين الوارد بعد الحظر وغيره. [فتح الغفار: 40،39] بعد الحظر أي بعد أن يكون المأمور به محظورًا ممنوعا قبل الأمر. أنه
Section on the Specific: Explanation of What the Imperative Indicates (Mūjib al-Amr). According to the majority (al-‘āmmah) it indicates obligation, not recommendation as some held, nor permission as some held, nor suspension as some held, nor shared meaning (ishtirāk) in wording or meaning between the three or two as others held. The author did not mention it because it is understood from what he mentioned implicitly. The people of recommendation (ahl al-nadb) say: The imperative is for a request, so the side of the action must be preponderant in order to request it, and its lowest degree is recommendation. This is like His saying the Exalted: '…contract with them if you know good in them…' (al-Nūr: 33). The people of permission (ahl al-ibāḥa) say: The meaning of request is that it be permitted and not forbidden, and its lowest degree is permission, as held by some, namely Abu Hashim and most of the Mu‘tazila, and it is reported from al-Shāfi‘ī in one saying. And not permission as transmitted from some followers of Mālik. The people of suspension (ahl al-tawaqquf) held by some, namely Abu al-‘Abbās Aḥmad ibn Shurayḥ from the followers of al-Shāfi‘ī. Then know that tawaqquf according to him is in determining the intended meaning at the time of usage, as indicated by the commentator’s statement later, so one must suspend judgment, not in determining the lexical meaning, for according to him the imperative is lexically a homonym (ishtirāk lafẓī) for obligation, recommendation, permission, and threat. 'And not shared in wording etc.' Know first that lexical homonymy means that a word is lexically assigned to each of the meanings individually, and meaningful homonymy (ishtirāk ma‘nawī) means that a word is lexically assigned to a single universal meaning that has individuals. Second, it is reported from al-Shāfi‘ī that it is a lexical homonym between obligation and recommendation. And it is transmitted from Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī that it is assigned for demand (iqtidā’), whether definite (hatman) or recommendatory (nadban), so it becomes a meaningful homonym between them. And it is said it is a lexical homonym between obligation, recommendation, and permission. And it is said it is a meaningful homonym among these three by being assigned for permission (idhn) that encompasses these three, which is the madhhab of al-Murtaḍā from the Shī‘a. 'Because it is understood etc.' - since he negated that recommendation and permission are what the imperative indicates, it is understood that it is not shared between two or three. And when he said that what the imperative indicates is obligation, it is understood that it is not a meaningful homonym among three or two, since on the first its indicated meaning is permission, and on the second its indicated meaning is demand as just discussed. 'And its lowest is recommendation' - because in permission the two sides are equal, while prohibition from leaving as in obligation is an extra element beyond preponderance. 'And its lowest is recommendation etc.' We say: the imperative is a request, so its absolute form directs to the complete one, and that is obligation. 'So contract with them etc.' Allah the Exalted said: 'And those who seek a contract from among those whom your right hands possess, then contract with them if you know good in them' (al-Nūr: 33) i.e., uprightness and ability to pay the māl of the kitāba, which is that the master says to his slave: I contract with you for such and such money; if he pays it, he is freed, and if anything remains on him, he remains a slave. So the command of kitāba here is for recommendation. And this is like His saying the Exalted: '…then hunt…' (al-Mā’ida: 2) so hunting is permissible. And the suspenders (mutawaqqifūn) say: The imperative is used for sixteen meanings, like obligation, permission, recommendation, threat, incapacitation, guidance, subjugation, and others. So as long as no qarīna indicates one of them, no action is taken, so one must suspend until the intended meaning is determined. And according to us, obligation is the literal meaning of the imperative, so its absolute form is interpreted accordingly unless a qarīna to the contrary exists. When such a qarīna exists, it is interpreted accordingly based on the context. Whether it occurs after a prohibition or not, this relates to his statement 'and its indicated meaning is obligation', and it is a refutation of those who say that the imperative after prohibition indicates permission, and before it indicates obligation according to what reason and custom require. Sixteen meanings: (1) obligation like 'Establish prayer' (al-An‘ām: 72); (2) permission as just mentioned; (3) recommendation as previously; (4) threat, which is addressing the other in anger like 'Do whatever you will' (Fuṣṣilat: 40) and similar to it is warning, though they made it another category, which is conveying with intimidation like 'Say: enjoy your disbelief a little' (al-Zumar: 8); (5) incapacitation like 'Then bring a chapter like it' (al-Baqara: 23); (6) guidance like 'And call to witness two just men among you' (al-Ṭalāq: 2), which is close to recommendation except it relates to worldly interests, while recommendation is for otherworldly reward; (7) subjugation like 'Be apes, despised' (al-Baqara: 65); (8) bestowal of favor (imtinān) like 'And eat from what Allah has provided you' (al-An‘ām: 142), for this command is for bestowal due to the qarīna of His saying '…from what Allah has provided you'; (9) honor (ikrām) like His saying 'Enter it in peace, secure' (al-Ḥijr: 46), for the context is a qarīna that the command is for honor; (10) humiliation as you say to one you humiliate 'Taste'; (11) equal treatment like His saying 'So be patient or not be patient' (al-Ṭūr: 16); (12) supplication like 'O Allah, forgive me'; (13) wishing like 'O Mālik, let your Lord make an end of us' (al-Zukhruf: 77); (14) belittlement like Moses' speech to the sorcerers of Pharaoh belittling them: 'Throw what you will throw' (Yūnus: 80); (15) creation (takwīn) like 'Be'; (16) discipline like hadith 'Eat from what is before you', which is close to recommendation except recommendation is for otherworldly reward and discipline for refining morals and improving habits. 'Obligation is the literal meaning of the imperative etc.' By obligation (wujūb) is meant bindingness (luzūm), and it is the linguistic wujūb, not the juristic, so it includes the definitively obligatory (wājib qaṭ‘ī) and the conjectural (ẓannī), because among the individuals of the imperative is what is established by a solitary report, and it is conjectural. If it were restricted to the qur’ānic imperative, its meaning would be definitive bindingness, not that which includes both, as in 'Mishkāt al-Anwār'. 'Whether after prohibition etc.' is an explanation of the chosen madhhab according to us, which is that it indicates obligation after prohibition or before it, due to the previous proofs, for they do not differentiate between coming after prohibition and other cases. [Fatḥ al-Ghaffār: 39-40] 'After prohibition' means after the commanded thing was prohibited and forbidden before the command. That it.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Anwar discusses a foundational usul issue: the indicative meaning (mūjib) of the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr). The author presents multiple positions from different groups and then establishes the Hanafi stance. The passage begins by stating the majority ('āmmah) view that the imperative indicates obligation (wujūb), as opposed to several other views: some hold it indicates recommendation (nadb), others permission (ibāḥa), others suspension (tawaqquf), and others that the imperative is a homonym (ishtirāk) either in wording or meaning between two or three of these categories. The author explains that the matn author did not mention the latter view because it is implicitly understood from his negation of the others. The people of recommendation argue that the imperative is a request, so the action must be preponderant, and the lowest degree of preponderance is recommendation. They cite the verse on kitāba (contract of manumission) as an example of a command that is recommendatory. The people of permission argue that the request means the action is permitted, so its lowest degree is permission; this is attributed to Abu Hashim and most Mu'tazila, and also reported from al-Shāfi'ī in one saying. The people of suspension, attributed to Abu al-'Abbas ibn Shurayḥ of the Shāfi'ī school, argue that the imperative is used for sixteen meanings, so one must suspend judgment until a qarīna (contextual indicator) specifies the intended meaning. They hold that the imperative is lexically a homonym for those meanings. The author then clarifies the two types of ishtirāk: lafẓī (word is assigned to multiple unrelated meanings) and ma'nawī (word is assigned to a single general meaning that includes individuals). Various reports are mentioned: al-Shāfi'ī holding lexical homonymy between obligation and recommendation; al-Māturīdī holding that it is assigned for demand (iqtidā') covering both definite and recommendatory, thus meaningful homonymy; others include permission as well, either lexically or meaningfully. The Hanafi position is then stated: the imperative is a ḥaqīqah (literal truth) for obligation, meaning bindingness (luzūm) in a linguistic sense, not just juristic wujūb. This encompasses both definitive and conjectural obligations because even commands from solitary reports are binding. The absolute imperative is interpreted as obligation unless a qarīna indicates otherwise. Importantly, this applies whether the command comes after a prohibition or not, contrary to some who differentiate. The passage ends with a list of sixteen possible meanings of the imperative form as found in the Quran and Sunnah, showing that while it can be used for many purposes (e.g., threat, incapacitation, guidance, etc.), the default in the absence of a qarīna is obligation. The Hanafi position is that the imperative is not a homonym but a literal truth for obligation, and other meanings are metaphorical (majāz) requiring a qarīna.

Key Terms
مُوجِب الأمر (mūjib al-amr): The ruling or meaning that the imperative form (ṣīghat al-amr) indicates in its absolute usage; according to the Hanafis, it is obligation (wujūb).
الاشتراك اللفظي (ishtirāk lafẓī): A single word lexically assigned to multiple distinct meanings without any shared core; e.g., 'ayn for eye, spring, etc.
الاشتراك المعنوي (ishtirāk ma‘nawī): A word lexically assigned to a single general meaning that includes individual sub-types; e.g., 'demand' (iqtidā’) covering both definite and recommended commands.
قرينة (qarīna): A contextual indicator that shifts the meaning of an imperative from its literal obligation to another meaning such as recommendation or permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the default ruling of an absolute imperative (without qarīna) that appears after a prohibition?
A) It indicates permission (ibāḥa) because it came after a prohibition.
B) It indicates recommendation (nadb) to be cautious.
C) It indicates obligation (wujūb) whether after prohibition or not.
D) It requires suspension (tawaqquf) until further evidence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the imperative form is literally for obligation (wujūb) in its absolute usage, and other meanings require a contextual indicator (qarīna); this principle is foundational for deriving rulings from commands in the Quran and Sunnah.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
15 min
The Literal Meaning of Command (Amr) Is Obligation (Wujub)
Analyzing the Hanafi ruling that the literal meaning of 'amr' is obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the literal meaning of amr is wujub, and evaluate the Shafi'i objection regarding commands after prohibition.

Core Idea
The command (amr) literally indicates obligation unless a qarina overrides it—negation of choice is the textual proof.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar establishes the fundamental Hanafi usul principle that the command (amr) is haqiqah for wujub. It refutes the Mutawaqqifun who suspend judgment, addresses the Shafi'i position on commands after prohibition, and provides the Quranic proof from al-Ahzab 33:36. Understanding this principle is key to interpreting Quranic and prophetic commands correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim about amr
State the Hanafi position: obligation is the literal meaning of the command, and it is carried on its absolute form until a qarina indicates otherwise.
This is the core thesis.
2
Contrast the Mutawaqqifun position
The suspensionists claim amr has sixteen possible meanings, so one must wait for a qarina. The Hanafis reject this as violating the principle of istishab.
The sixteen meanings are listed in the text.
3
Analyze the proof from negation of choice
Examine al-Ahzab 33:36 which negates choice (khiyara) for believers when Allah and His Messenger decree something. Since choice is a necessary concomitant of permission and recommendation, negating it negates those meanings, leaving obligation.
This is the main textual dalil.
4
Apply to commands after prohibition
Consider the example of hunting after ihram. The Hanafi view: the command itself still indicates obligation, but permissibility comes from separate qarinat (e.g., explicit permission).
Contrast with the Shafi'i view that such commands directly indicate ibaha.
5
Evaluate the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i use the hunting example. The Hanafi response: the qarina (verbal and rational) diverts from obligation, but the default remains obligation. The prohibition itself does not constitute a qarina.
This leads to the thamarah al-khilaf in interpretation.

كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِذَا حَلَلْتُمْ فَاصْطَادُوا، ونحن نقول: إن الوجوب بعد الحظر أيضا
(المائدة : ٢ ) .

ثم شرع في بيان دلائل الوجوب فقال لانتفاء الخيرة عن المأمور بالأمر بالنص أي إنما قلنا: إن موجبه الوجوب؛ لانتفاء الاختيار عن المأمورين المكلفين بالأمر بالنص، وهو قوله
النص
تعالى: وَمَا كَانَ لِمُؤْمِنٍ وَلَا مُؤْمِنَةٍ إِذَا قَضَى اللَّهُ وَرَسُولُهُ أَمْراً أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُمُ الْخِيَرَةُ مِنْ أَمْرِهِمْ ؟ (الأحزاب : ٣٦)
وإذا حللتم إلخ: أي إذا خرجتم من الإحرام فاصطادوا، فالاصطياد كان حلالاً مباحًا، ثم حرم بسبب الإحرام، فكان قوله تعالى: فَاصْطَادُوا (المائدة: (۲) إعلاما بأن سبب التحريم قد ارتفع وعاد الأمر إلى أصله. (القمر) أيضًا مستعمل إلخ فالحظر المتقدم على الأمر لا يصلح قرينة لصرفه عن الوجوب إلى الإباحة. (القمر) الأشهر الحرم وهي أربعة رجب وذو القعدة وذو الحجة والمحرم فالقتال في هذه الأشهر كان محظورًا ممنوعا ثم ثبت وجوبه. (القمر) والإباحة إلخ جواب عن مثال الخصم. (القمر)
لانتفاء الخيرة إلخ والخيرة من لوازم الندب ،والإباحة، فإذا انتفت انتفيا، والخيرة بكسر الأول وفتح الثاني الاختيار وقوله عن المأمور وقوله بالنص متعلقان بالانتفاء وتعلق الثاني بعد تقييد الانتفاء بالأول، وقوله: بالأمر
متعلق بالمأمور، وما في "مشكاة الأنوار" من أن بالأمر متعلق بالوجوب فشطط لا تلتفت إليه. (القمر) إنما قلنا إلخ إيماء إلى أن الجار في لانتفاء الخيرة إلخ متعلق بقوله : "وموجبه الوجوب إلخ". (القمر) لانتفاء الاختيار إلخ أي الاختيار الذي هو من لوازم الإباحة والندب، وإذا انتفى اللازم انتفى الملزوم، وثبت الوجوب لثبوت ملزومه، وهو انتفاء الاختيار. (السنبلي)
لهم: الضمير راجع إلى المؤمن والمؤمنة، وإنما جمع لعمومهما من حيث أنهما في سياق النفي. (القمر)
من
أمرهم: الضمير راجع إلى الله ورسوله، وإنما جمع للتعظيم (القمر)
Section on the Particular (al-Khass): Explanation of the implication of the command. This is like His saying: 'then hunt' (al-Ma'idah: 2). The Mutaqifun (suspensionists) say that the command is used for sixteen meanings like obligation, permission, recommendation, threat, incapacitation, guidance, subjection, and others. So as long as no contextual indicator (qarina) is established for one of them, it cannot be acted upon; rather, suspension is necessary until the intended meaning is determined. According to us, obligation is the literal meaning of the command, so it is carried upon its absolute form as long as no qarina to the contrary exists, and if a qarina exists, it is carried according to the context. Whether it is after prohibition or before, related to his statement: 'And its implication is obligation', and it is a refutation of those who say from the Shafi'is that the command after prohibition indicates permission, and before prohibition indicates obligation according to what reason and custom dictate. The sixteen meanings: (1) obligation like 'establish prayer' (al-An'am: 72), (2) permission as just mentioned, (3) recommendation as previously, (4) threat which is addressing another with anger like 'do what you will' (Fussilat: 40), and near it is warning though they make another category which is informing with fright like 'and say enjoy your disbelief a little' (az-Zumar: 8), (5) incapacitation like 'then bring a surah like it' (al-Baqarah: 23), (6) guidance like 'and call to witness two just persons among you' (at-Talaq: 2) which is near recommendation except that it pertains to worldly interests while recommendation for otherworldly reward. (7) subjection like 'be apes, despised' (al-Baqarah: 65), (8) bestowal of favor like 'and eat from what Allah has provided you' (al-An'am: 142), for this command is for bestowal of favor due to the qarina of His saying 'and eat from what Allah has provided you' (al-An'am: 142), (9) honoring like His saying 'enter it in peace, secure' (al-Hijr: 46), for the context is a qarina that the command is for honoring, (10) humiliation as you say to one whom you humiliate 'taste', (11) equalization like His saying 'so be patient or not be patient' (at-Tur: 16), (12) supplication like 'O Allah, forgive me', (13) wishing like 'O Malik, let your Lord finish us off' (az-Zukhruf: 77), (14) belittlement like the saying of Musa to the magicians of Pharaoh belittling them: 'Throw what you are going to throw' (Yunus: 80), (15) formation like 'be', (16) discipline like his statement to Ibn Abbas 'eat from what is in front of you' – which is near recommendation except that recommendation is for otherworldly reward and discipline for refining morals and correcting habits. (al-Qamar) 'Obligation is the literal meaning of the command etc.': what is meant by obligation is necessity, and lexical obligation not juristic, so it includes both definitive and speculative obligation; because among instances of the command are those established by khabar wahid which is speculative, and if it were restricted to the Qur'anic command it would mean definitive necessity not what includes both, as in Mishkat al-Anwar. (al-Qamar) 'Whether it is after prohibition etc.': explanation of the chosen madhhab according to us, which is that it is for obligation after prohibition or before, due to the previous proofs, for they do not differentiate between what occurs after prohibition and other than it. [Fath al-Ghaffar: 39-40] 'After prohibition' i.e. after the commanded thing was prohibited and forbidden before the command. (al-Qamar) That it is also used in the Qur'an like His saying: 'and when you have completed the rites of Hajj then hunt' – and we say: obligation after prohibition is also used in the Qur'an like His saying: 'then when the sacred months have passed, kill the polytheists wherever you find them' (at-Tawbah: 5). And the permissibility in His saying 'and when you have completed the rites of Hajj then hunt' was not understood from the command but from His saying 'permitted for you are good things' (al-Ma'idah: 5) – this is a verbal qarina – and from the fact that the command to hunt was a favor and benefit for the servants – this is a rational qarina. And if it were an obligation it would be a hardship upon them. So the command when absolute is for obligation, and it is only carried upon other meanings with qarinat and majaz. Then he proceeded to explain the proofs of obligation, saying: 'Due to the negation of choice (al-khiyara) from the one commanded by the command, by the text' i.e. we only say that its implication is obligation because of the negation of choice for the obligated persons commanded by the command, by the text, which is His saying: 'It is not for a believing man or a believing woman, when Allah and His Messenger have decreed a matter, that they should have any choice in their matter' (al-Ahzab: 36). 'And when you have completed etc.': i.e. when you exit from ihram then hunt; hunting was lawful/permissible, then it became prohibited due to ihram, so His saying 'then hunt' (al-Ma'idah: 2) indicated that the cause of prohibition has been removed and the matter returned to its original state. (al-Qamar) Also 'it is used etc.': so the preceding prohibition does not serve as a qarina to divert it from obligation to permission. (al-Qamar) 'The sacred months' – they are four: Rajab, Dhu al-Qa'dah, Dhu al-Hijjah, and Muharram; so fighting in these months was prohibited/forbidden, then its obligation was established. (al-Qamar) 'And the permissibility etc.': a response to the opponent's example. (al-Qamar) 'Due to the negation of choice etc.': and choice is a necessary concomitant of recommendation and permission, so if they are negated they are negated, and 'al-khiyara' with kasra of the first letter and fatha of the second means 'choice'. And his statement 'from the one commanded' and his statement 'by the text' are both related to 'negation', and the second is attached after restricting the negation with the first. And his statement 'by the command' is related to 'the one commanded'. And what is in Mishkat al-Anwar that 'by the command' is related to 'obligation' is excessive, do not pay attention to it. (al-Qamar) 'We only say etc.': indicates that the prepositional phrase in 'due to the negation of choice' etc. is related to his statement 'and its implication is obligation' etc. (al-Qamar) 'Due to the negation of choice etc.': i.e. the choice which is a necessary concomitant of permission and recommendation; when the necessary concomitant is negated, the accompanied thing is negated, and obligation is established due to the establishment of its necessary concomitant, which is the negation of choice. (al-Sanabili) 'For them': the pronoun refers to the believing man and believing woman, and it is plural because of their generality in the context of negation. (al-Qamar) 'Of their matter': the pronoun refers to Allah and His Messenger, and it is plural for glorification. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Hanafi ruling on the implication of the command (amr). It begins by stating the position of the 'Mutawaqqifun' (those who suspend judgment) who claim that the command can mean any of sixteen different meanings, and therefore one must suspend judgment until a contextual indicator (qarina) specifies the intention. The Hanafi school rejects this and asserts that obligation (wujub) is the literal (haqiqi) meaning of the command; the command is to be understood as obligating action unless there is a qarina indicating otherwise. The text then elaborates that this principle applies regardless of whether the command comes after a prohibition or not. This directly counters the Shafi'i view (attributed to some Shafi'is) that a command after a prohibition indicates permission (ibaha), while a command before a prohibition indicates obligation. The author then lists sixteen possible meanings of the command, providing Quranic and prophetic examples for each, such as obligation, permission, recommendation, threat, incapacitation, guidance, subjection, bestowal of favor, honoring, humiliation, equalization, supplication, wishing, belittlement, formation, and discipline. The commentary (al-Qamar) clarifies that the 'obligation' intended here is linguistic (luzum), not the juristic category of fard or wajib; it includes both definitive (qat'i) and speculative (zanni) obligations because the command can come through ahad reports. The text then addresses the specific issue of commands after prohibition, using the example of hunting after ihram: the command 'then hunt' after the prohibition during ihram is not for obligation but for permission, but the Hanafi position is that the command itself remains for obligation, and the permissibility is inferred from other contexts (such as the explicit statement 'permitted for you are good things' and rational considerations). The final part of the passage presents the central proof for obligation: the negation of choice (khiyara) from the commanded. The Quranic verse (al-Ahzab 33:36) states that believers have no choice when Allah and His Messenger decree a matter. Since choice is a necessary concomitant of recommendation and permission, its negation proves that the command implies obligation. The commentary further refines the grammatical and logical points, emphasizing that the negation of choice necessarily negates the accompanying meanings, leaving only obligation.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The command; a verbal form demanding an action from a superior to an inferior. In Hanafi usul, its literal meaning is obligation, but it can be used metaphorically for other meanings with a qarina.
الوجوب (al-wujub): Linguistic necessity (luzum) that includes both definitive (qat'i) and speculative (zanni) obligation. Here it is not the juristic fard but the general sense of being required.
القرينة (al-qarina): A contextual indicator, either verbal (lafziyya) or rational (aqliyya), that diverts the command from its literal meaning of obligation to another meaning.
الخيرة (al-khiyara): Choice or option; a necessary concomitant (lazim) of permission (ibaha) and recommendation (nadb). The negation of choice proves the negation of these meanings and thus establishes obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the role of qarina (contextual indicator) in interpreting a command?
A) It is always required to determine the meaning.
B) It is only needed to divert from the literal meaning of obligation.
C) It is never considered.
D) It only applies to commands after prohibition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the literal meaning of a command is obligation, and any other meaning requires a contextual indicator (qarina) to override that default.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

8 min
Ism al-Fa'il and Non-Repetition: The Divorce and Command Example
Textual analysis of ism al-fa'il and amr non-repetition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that the active participle (ism al-fāʿil) and the command form (al-amr) do not imply repetition, and apply it to the divorce scenario where a husband says 'ṭalliqī nafsak' to determine the number of divorces.

Core Idea
Active participles and commands indicate a single instance, not repetition.

This passage clarifies a foundational usul principle: the active participle, like the command, does not imply repetition or number. This linguistic observation has direct fiqh consequences. For example, if a husband tells his wife 'divorce yourself', how many divorces can she execute? The Hanafi school restricts it to one, while al-Shāfiʿī allows more if intended. Understanding the linguistic basis helps resolve such disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parallel
Recognize that the author draws an analogy between the command (al-amr) and the active participle (ism al-fāʿil): both are inherently non-repetitive.
2
State the Hanafi principle
According to Hanafis, neither the command nor the active participle implies multiple occurrences. Repetition is achieved only through repeated causes or renewed commands.
3
Apply to the divorce scenario
Analyze the husband's statement 'ṭalliqī nafsak' (divorce yourself). Under the Hanafi view, since the command does not imply repetition, the wife may execute only one divorce, regardless of the husband's intention.
Compare with al-Shāfiʿī who allows two if the husband intended two.
4
Extend to the active participle
The same principle applies to active participles like 'sāriq' (thief). The word indicates the act of stealing without implying multiple thefts.
5
Derive the fiqh ruling
Conclude that the Qur'anic command to cut the thief's hand refers to a single theft, and analogously, the divorce command yields a single divorce.

مبحث الخاص
بيان اسم الفاعل مثل الأمر لا يحتمل التكرار فكيف يكون السبب مغنيا عن الأمر؛ لأنا نقول: إن عند وجود كل سبب يتكرر الأمر تقديرًا من جانب الله تعالى، فكان تكرار العبادات بتكرر الأوامر المتجددة حكمًا. وعند الشافعي له لما احتمل التكرار تملك أن تطلق نفسها ثنتين إذا نوى الزوج، بيان لخلاف الشافعي في أصل كلي على وجه يتضمن الخلاف في المسألة المذكورة يعني أن عنده لما احتمل كل أمر التكرار ، سواء كان أمر الشارع أو غيره تملك المرأة في قوله: "طلقي نفسك" أن تطلق نفسها ثنتين إذا نوى الزوج ذلك وإن لم ينو، أو نوى واحدة، فلها أن تطلق نفسها واحدة.
ثم أورد المصنف تقريب بيان الأمر بيان اسم الفاعل؛ لاشتراكهما في عدم احتمال التكرار، فقال: وكذا اسم الفاعل يدل على المصدر لغة ولا يحتمل العدد، فقوله: "يدل" بيان لوجه التشبيه، ولا يحتمل عطف عليه، وفي بعض النسخ: لا يحتمل بدون الواو، فيكون هو بيان وجه التشبيه، وقوله : يدل وقع حالاً أي كذا اسم الفاعل لا يحتمل العدد حال كونه يدل على المصدر لغة، فهو احتراز عن اسم الفاعل الذي يدل عليه اقتضاء مثل قوله : " أنت طالق"، فإنه خارج عما نحن فيه، وسيأتي بيانه حتى لا يُراد بآية السرقة إلا سرقة واحدة وبالفعل الواحد لا تقطع إلا يد واحدة تفريع على عدم احتمال اسم الفاعل التكرار وإلزام على الشافعي له فيما ذهب إليه، بيانه: أن الشافعي يقول: إن السارق تقطع يده اليمنى أولاً، ثم رجله اليسرى ثانيا، ثم يده اليسرى ثالثًا، ثم رجله اليمنى رابعًا؛ لقوله عليه السلام من سرق فاقطعوه فإن عاد فاقطعوه، فإن عاد فاقطعوه، فإن عاد فاقطعوه، وعندنا لا تقطع اليد اليسرى في الثالثة، بل خلّد في السجن حتى يتوب؛ لأن السارق اسم الفاعل يدل على المصدر لغة والمصدر لا يراد به إلا الواحد أو الكل، الحقيقي
يدل عليه أي على المصدر اقتضاء إلخ، فإن الطالق إنما يدل لغة على طلاق يكون صفة للمرأة لا على طلاق يكون بمعنى التطليق كالسلام بمعنى التسليم، وفعل الرجل هو التطليق لا الأول، فإن الأول وصف ضروري تتصف به المرأة لكن الطالق يدل على التطليق اقتضاء فهو ثابت شرعًا ضرورة تصحيح هذا الكلام أي وصف الزوج إياها بالطلاق الإخباري كذا في "العناية، ومن ههنا اتضح ما قال الشارح في "المنهية"، فإن الطلاق المفهوم بحسب اللغة من في ضمن قوله : أنت طالق هو الطلاق الذي هو وصف المرأة لا التطليق الذي هو فعل الزوج انتهت. (القمر) فإنه خارج عما نحن فيه: فإنه لا يقع بقوله: "أنت طالق" إلا واحد وإن نوى أكثر من ذلك كذا في "الهداية". (القمر) ثم رجله اليسرى ثانيًا : هذا بالإجماع إما من الكعب على ما فعل عمر ، وعليه أكثر أهل العلم، أو من نصف القدم معقد الشراك على ما فعل عليه السلام وأودع له عقب يمشي عليه كذا نقل في بعض الحواشي عن "فتح القدير". (القمر) من سرق إلخ قال الشيخ ابن الهمام: إن الحديث بهذا اللفظ لا يعرف وأخرج الدار قطني مفسراً قال قال رسول الله : إذا سرق السارق فاقطعوا يده فان عاد فاقطعوا رجله فان عاد فاقطعوا يده فان عاد فاقطعوا رجله، وههنا طرق كثيرة متعددة لم تسلم من الطعن، وقال الطحاوي: تتبعنا هذه الآثار فلم نجد الشيء منها أثرا، وفي "المبسوط". الحديث غير صحيح، وإلا احتج به بعضهم في مشاورة عليه حين قال: إني لأستحي من الله تعالى أن لا أدع له يدا يأكل بها، ويستنجي بها، ورجلا يمشي عليها، وبهذا حاج بقية الصحابة منه، فحجهم فانعقد إجماعًا، ولئن سلم أن الحديث صحيح، فيحمل على الانتساخ ؛ لأنه كان في الابتداء تغليظ في الحدود، ألا ترى أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قطع أيدي العربيين وأرجلهم وسمر أعينهم، ثم انتسخ ذلك فتأمل (القمر) هذا الحديث بهذا اللفظ ،غريب وأخرج الدار قطني في "سننه" ، رقم ،۲۹۲ ، ۱۸۱/۳ ، بلفظ: "إذا سرق السارق فاقطعوا يده، فإن عاد فاقطعوا رجله، فإن عاد فاقطعوا يده، فإن عاد فاقطعوا رجله" عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه
Section on the Specific. Explanation that Ism al-Fa'il is like the command in not implying repetition. So how can the cause suffice for the command? We say: Indeed, upon the occurrence of every cause, the command is repeated virtually from Allah Almighty, so the repetition of acts of worship is by the repetition of renewed commands by consideration. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, because it admitted repetition, the woman has the right to divorce herself twice if the husband intended, explanation of the disagreement with al-Shafi'i on a universal principle that includes the disagreement in the mentioned issue, meaning that according to him, when every command admits repetition, whether the command of the Lawgiver or otherwise, the woman in his saying "Divorce yourself" has the right to divorce herself twice if the husband intended that, and if he did not intend, or intended one, she may divorce herself once. Then the author brought an approximation of the explanation of the command to the explanation of Ism al-Fa'il, because they share in not admitting repetition. He said: Likewise Ism al-Fa'il indicates the masdar linguistically and does not admit number. So his saying "indicates" is an explanation of the aspect of similarity, and "does not admit" is coordinated with it. In some copies: "does not admit" without the waw, so it is the explanation of the aspect of similarity. His saying "indicates" occurs as a circumstantial accusative, i.e., likewise Ism al-Fa'il does not admit number while it indicates the masdar linguistically. This is a restriction against Ism al-Fa'il which indicates by requirement like his saying "You are divorced", for it is outside what we are in, and its explanation will come, so that by the verse of theft is intended only one theft, and by one act only one hand is cut, as a branch on the non-admission of repetition by Ism al-Fa'il, and as a refutation against al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, in what he went to. Its explanation: al-Shafi'i says that the thief's right hand is cut first, then his left foot second, then his left hand third, then his right foot fourth, because of his saying, peace be upon him: "Whoever steals, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot; if he returns, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot." According to us, the left hand is not cut in the third, but he is imprisoned until he repents, because “al-sāriq” is an active participle that indicates the masdar linguistically, and the masdar is not intended except as one or the whole. Real. It indicates it, i.e., the masdar, by requirement etc. For “al-ṭāliq” only indicates linguistically a divorce that is an attribute of the woman, not a divorce in the sense of tatlīq, like “salām” in the sense of taslīm. The act of the man is tatlīq, not the first, for the first is a necessary attribute that the woman is described with, but “al-ṭāliq” indicates tatlīq by requirement, so it is established legally by necessity of correcting this speech, i.e., describing the husband her with the predicative divorce. As in al-ʿInāya. From here it is clear what the commentator said in al-Munyat [؟], for the divorce understood linguistically from within his saying “You are divorced” is the divorce that is the attribute of the woman, not the tatlīq which is the act of the husband. End. (al-Qamar) It is outside what we are in: For by his saying “You are divorced” only one occurs even if he intends more than that. As in al-Hidāya. (al-Qamar) Then his left foot second: This is by consensus, either from the ankle as ʿUmar did, and upon it most scholars, or from half of the foot at the knot of the strap as the Prophet did and He placed for him a heel to walk on. As quoted in some marginalia from Fatḥ al-Qadīr. (al-Qamar) “Whoever steals” etc. Shaykh Ibn al-Humām said: The hadith with this wording is not known. Al-Dāraquṭnī narrated it with explanation: The Messenger of Allah said: “If the thief steals, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot; if he returns, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot.” Here there are many multiple chains that are not free from criticism. Al-Ṭaḥāwī said: We followed these reports and did not find any of them to be an authentic report. In al-Mabsūṭ, the hadith is not authentic. Otherwise, some would have used it in consultation when he said: I am shy before Allah to leave him a hand to eat with and clean himself with, and a foot to walk on. By this, the rest of the Companions argued with him, and he defeated them through argument, so consensus was established. And if it is accepted that the hadith is authentic, it is interpreted as abrogated, because at the beginning there was severity in the penalties. Do you not see that the Prophet cut off the hands and feet of the two Arabs and gouged out their eyes, then that was abrogated? So ponder. (al-Qamar) This hadith with this wording is strange. Al-Dāraquṭnī narrated in his Sunan, vol. 3, p. 181, no. 292, with the wording: "If the thief steals, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot; if he returns, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot" from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis demonstrating that the active participle (ism al-fāʿil), like the command form (al-amr), does not linguistically imply repetition. The author begins by contrasting the Hanafi view that the cause for an obligation (e.g., the entry of prayer time) triggers a renewed divine command each time, so the repetition of worship is due to repeated commands. Against this, al-Shāfiʿī holds that a single command can admit repetition, leading to a consequence in the divorce example: if a husband says to his wife “ṭalliqī nafsak” (divorce yourself), and he intends two divorces, she may execute two, whereas if he intends one or has no intention, she executes only one. The author then draws a parallel between the command and the active participle: both do not admit number/repetition. The active participle (e.g., sāriq – thief) indicates the masdar (action) linguistically and does not imply multiplicity. Thus, the Qur’ānic verse on theft (al-Mā’ida 5:38) is understood to prescribe cutting only one hand for a single theft. The Hanafīs apply this to the punishment of the repeat thief: they cut the right hand first, but if he repeats, they do not cut further hands or feet; instead, he is imprisoned until repentance. This opposes al-Shāfiʿī, who cuts hand, then foot, then hand, then foot based on a hadith. The Hanafīs weaken the hadith, citing that it is not authentic, and even if authentic, it is abrogated by later practice and consensus. The passage also clarifies that ism al-fāʿil used in other contexts, like ṭāliq (divorced woman), indicates the attribute, not the act of divorcing, but by requirement it implies the act legally.

Key Terms
اسم الفاعل (ism al-fāʿil): An active participle that indicates the masdar (action) linguistically and does not imply repetition.
تكرار (takrār): Repetition; the active participle does not admit number or repetition by its linguistic nature.
الخاص (al-khāṣ): The specific; the section deals with specific (non-repetitive) meanings of active participles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view discussed, what is the ruling when a husband says 'ṭalliqī nafsak' and intends two divorces?
A) The wife may execute two divorces
B) The wife may execute one divorce only
C) The wife may execute three divorces
D) The divorce is invalid

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that the command and active participle do not imply repetition restricts the divorce to a single instance and forms the basis for the theft punishment rule.

The Theft Punishment Dispute: Ism al-Fa'il and Repetition
Textual analysis and inter-madhhab dispute on theft punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi and Shafi'i arguments regarding the punishment for repeat theft and identify the role of the active participle principle in the Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
The active participle 'sāriq' limits the theft verse to one hand cutting, blocking sequential amputations.

This passage applies the linguistic principle that the active participle does not imply repetition to a major fiqh dispute: the punishment for a repeat thief. The Hanafi school, relying on this principle, holds that only the right hand is cut for the first theft. For repeated thefts, the criminal is imprisoned. The Shafi'i school, citing a hadith, cuts hand, foot, hand, and foot in sequence. The Hanafi response weakens the hadith and argues for consensus and abrogation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
For the first theft, cut the right hand. For subsequent thefts, imprison until repentance. Do not cut additional limbs.
2
Identify the linguistic basis
The word 'sāriq' (thief) in the Qur'an is an active participle. It indicates the act of stealing without implying multiplicity, so the command to cut the hand applies only once.
3
Present the Shafi'i position and proof
Shafi'is cut the right hand, then left foot, then left hand, then right foot for repeated thefts. Their proof is a hadith: 'If he steals, cut his hand; if he returns, cut his foot...'
The hadith is reported by al-Daraqutni.
4
Analyze the Hanafi critique of the hadith
The Hanafis argue the hadith is not authentic (da'if). Even if accepted, they claim it is abrogated by the practice of the Companions and the Prophet's later action (gouging eyes was abrogated). Consensus (ijma) was reached on imprisonment.
5
Derive the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: for the third theft, Hanafis imprison; Shafi'is cut the left hand. This shows the impact of the linguistic principle.

And if it is accepted that the hadith is authentic, it is interpreted as abrogated, because at the beginning there was severity in the penalties. Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis demonstrating that the active participle (ism al-fāʿil), like the command form (al-amr), does not linguistically imply repetition. The author begins by contrasting the Hanafi view that the cause for an obligation (e.g., the entry of prayer time) triggers a renewed divine command each time, so the repetition of worship is due to repeated commands. The author then draws a parallel between the command and the active participle: both do not admit number/repetition. The active participle (e.g., sāriq – thief) indicates the masdar (action) linguistically and does not imply multiplicity. Thus, the Qur’ānic verse on theft (al-Mā’ida 5:38) is understood to prescribe cutting only one hand for a single theft. The Hanafīs apply this to the punishment of the repeat thief: they cut the right hand first, but if he repeats, they do not cut further hands or feet; instead, he is imprisoned until repentance. The Hanafīs weaken the hadith, citing that it is not authentic, and even if authentic, it is abrogated by later practice and consensus. Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Active Participle Does Not Imply Repetition: Theft Punishment
Textual analysis of ism fa'il implications for theft punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that ism fa'il does not imply repetition to derive the ruling on theft punishment, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using usul principles.

Core Idea
The active participle indicates a single action, so only one hand is cut per theft — not the left hand in repeated theft.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi usul principle: the active participle (ism fa'il) does not imply repetition. The passage applies this principle to the verse on theft (al-Ma'idah 5:38) to argue that only one hand is cut, and critiques the Shafi'i view that would cut the left hand in a third theft. You will learn how linguistic analysis, consensus (ijma), and hadith criticism combine to shape a ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic claim
Identify the claim that ism fa'il (like 'al-sariq') indicates the masdar (source) which is singular — thus it points to one theft, not repeated acts.
The example 'anta taliq' (you are divorced) is used to show that only one divorce occurs, even if more is intended.
2
Apply to the theft verse
Apply this claim to al-Ma'idah 5:38: the word 'al-sariq' limits the punishment to one theft per occurrence, so only one hand is cut for each theft.
The right hand is specifically intended by consensus and other evidence (e.g., hadith of Makhzumi woman, Ibn Mas'ud's reading).
3
Examine the Shafi'i objection
State the Shafi'i position: they cut left hand in third theft based on a hadith that commands cutting the hand again upon return.
The hadith: 'If the thief steals, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot; if he returns, cut off his hand; if he returns, cut off his foot.' (al-Daraqutni)
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: (1) the hadith is not authentic (Ibn al-Humam, al-Tahawi), (2) even if authentic, it is abrogated by later practice, (3) the left hand cannot be added by a solitary report because the verse specifies the right hand.
Also, the term 'faqt'u' (cut) does not accept number, and the locus (right hand) is removed after cutting.
5
Compare with flogging for zina
Understand the distinction: flogging can be repeated because the body remains a suitable locus, whereas the hand is cut once, so the locus is gone.
This refutes the objection that the same reasoning would apply to zina — it does not, due to different loci.
